Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,336 words
21 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B8
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Cybersecurity Sector Has Limits

Companies no longer are spending blindly on cybersecurity. Investors will have to start getting pickier as well.

For a time, money flowed easily to practically any company that came up with a new way to safeguard corporate networks. Venture capitalists invested nearly $3.8 billion in cybersecurity startups last year, up 69% from the year before, according to CB Insights. And established players also were doing well. A group of cybersecurity companies tracked by UBS improved revenue by an average of 35% last year.

But CB Insights says about $845 million of venture-capital investments went to cybersecurity companies in the third quarter, down 24% from a year earlier. Brent Thill of UBS expects those companies tracked by his firm to average growth of just 20% this year and 17% next year.

A recent warning by Fortinet suggests that forthcoming third-quarter reports will show more deceleration. The company, which specializes in firewalls and associated software, lowered its third-quarter forecast last week, as Chief Executive Ken Xie cited a mix of internal issues. He also noted that corporate customers were "buying with less urgency than last year."

It should be noted that security remains a priority for corporate executives. According to a recent survey by Citigroup, 79% of chief information officers plan to increase their spending on network security over the next 12 months, compared with 77% at the last survey. A similar survey by Morgan Stanley found that security software was the priority for CIOs, above analytics and cloud computing.

However, not all will benefit equally. Analysts say companies focused on firewalls and other hardware may see more price competition. Those that sell security as a service may benefit from a growing corporate preference for cloud-based offerings. But shifting to a service model tends to suppress near-term revenue as more is deferred to future periods. Palo Alto Networks, one of Wall Street's most favored names in security, is down 15% this year, as growth logged in its past two quarterly reports has reflected that shift.

One hopeful sign is that depressed valuations may spur some needed consolidation. Software M&A is already on a tear, with 1,401 announced deals targeting U.S. firms year to date, according to Dealogic. That is up 9% from a year earlier.

The Hack Cyber Security ETF by PureFunds is up less than 1% this year compared with a 8% gain for the S&P 500 Software & Services group. About one-third of the stocks in the Hack fund are down by double-digits for the year. For momentum-driven investors, cybersecurity has hardly been a lock.

-- Dan Gallagher

The Saudis' Mammoth Bond Is a Defining Deal for 2016

Want a single instrument that wraps together nearly every big political, financial and economic theme in today's world?

Saudi Arabia's mammoth $17.5 billion bond issue, marking its debut in international markets, is it.

The size of the deal is impressive but actually the least important thing about it. Big bond deals tend to build a momentum of their own.

But it does speak to the search for yield. The $6.5 billion 30-year portion of Saudi Arabia's bond is set to pay 2.1 percentage points more in yield than a comparable U.S. Treasury, or around 4.6%.

That is a sizable pickup in a world where developed-market bond yields are on the floor or in negative territory.

That Saudi Arabia is doing the deal at all is a more telling factor: The oil bounty that has propelled the economy has run dry.

The 18-month-long rout in oil prices that started in 2014 sent the country hurtling from a budget surplus to a deficit in 2015 of 15.9% of gross domestic product. That is set to narrow to only 13% in 2016, according to the International Monetary Fund.

In 2013, government debt stood at just 2.2% of GDP, according to Moody's. By 2017, it is forecast to climb to 22.9%.

The level isn't a source of concern, but the swift change illustrates the country's stark reversal of fortunes.

In the near term, buyers of the bonds are betting largely on oil. Swings in the price are likely to have a direct impact on the perception of Saudi Arabia as a credit.

The recovery in oil prices, which stand close to their high of the year, has eased concerns about financial stability and helps explain some of the enthusiasm for the deal.

But further ahead, this is a bet on the ability and willingness of the country to transform itself while maintaining social and political stability.

In that, the 30-year bond is much punchier than the five- and 10-year tranches that accompany it both in terms of domestic politics but also the future of the Middle East as a region. Saudi Arabia has set out ambitious plans to diversify away from oil, but they will take years to come to fruition.

Bankers sometimes like to talk in grandiose terms about their deals. For once, the labels might be justified. The Saudi deal is truly a landmark; its performance over time will say a lot about how the world is changing.

-- Richard Barley

Salesforce

Looks More

Grounded

For Salesforce.com, Twitter appears to have been an aberration. That is actually a bit comforting.

The Wall Street Journal reported leaked emails from Salesforce board member and former Secretary of State Colin Powell that show more than a dozen public companies Salesforce was considering as potential acquisition targets, as of mid-May. One was Demandware, which Salesforce bought in June for $2.8 billion.

Twitter was notably absent from the list. Salesforce reportedly began looking at the troubled social network last month, but it doesn't appear that Twitter was ever a serious part of its strategic plan. Salesforce shareholders recoiled at the idea anyway, sending the stock down 5% between the company's first reported interest and late last week, when it called off the effort.

But Salesforce was clearly in acquisition mode. And its wish list shows the company is opportunistic and more interested in playing close to home. CEO Marc Benioff spoke in his last earnings call about an "M&A window" created by a selloff in cloud and software stocks earlier in the year. The stocks of two companies highlighted in the emails ServiceNow and Tableau Software were down 20% and 50% for the year at the time.

Software valuations largely have rebounded, with the S&P 500 Software & Services Group up 9% since mid-May through Wednesday. So the same kind of bargains won't be as easy to come by. Still, it is nice to know that, when Salesforce puts its dreams to paper, most aren't likely to turn to nightmares.

-- Dan Gallagher

Overheard

Don't spend it all in one place. In fact, you had better not spend it at all.

U.S. Social Security recipients were told what sort of raise they will receive next year and, as expected, it isn't much. The average monthly pension check will go up by about $5 for the 60 million plus people who get them.

But around one-third of the 56 million people who get Medicare the health-insurance program for the elderly nearly all of whom also get Social Security, will see their premiums rise more.

Even for the remainder, who may still see a modest increase, deductibles are set to rise by an amount well above the boost to their checks.

It could be worse for today's senior citizens. According to a report earlier this year from Social Security trustees, those 49 or younger may never get what they are owed as the program will only be able to pay about three-quarters of projected benefits.

Pro Private Markets
Andreessen Horowitz Leads $2M Seed Financing for Cardiogram

By Brian Gormley 
359 words
20 October 2016
23:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Cardiogram Inc. has secured $2 million on the promise that its app could help more people learn they have irregular heartbeats before they're hit by a stroke or some complication of the heart condition.

Andreessen Horowitz is leading the seed financing, which also included individuals such as Rock Health co-founder Halle Tecco and Color Genomics Inc.'s co-founders Elad Gil and Othman Laraki.

Atrial fibrillation, an irregular heartbeat, can lead to a stroke or heart failure. About 2% of people younger than 65 and 9% of people aged 65 and older have atrial fibrillation, according to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. Atrial fibrillation can cause symptoms such as weakness and fatigue. Some people have no noticeable symptoms.

Cardiogram expects the combination of its app and the Apple Watch to improve heart health. The app organizes heart-rate data collected by the Apple Watch. Users can see their heart rate chart on the Apple Watch and get more detailed data and see historical trends on their iPhone. A new feature of the Cardiogram app called Habits enables users to see how specific actions, such as exercise, affect heart-rate data.

Through a study called mRhythm, Cardiogram aims to find out if its app can detect atrial fibrillation. Cardiogram is combining data from more than 100,000 users who have downloaded the app with data from atrial fibrillation patients undergoing a treatment known as cardioversion.

By using that data, Cardiogram intends to teach its machine-learning algorithm to correctly distinguish between normal and abnormal heart rhythms.

Venture investment in products for the heart reached $563.4 million in the first half, according to market tracker Dow Jones VentureSource. That puts this year on track to exceed the $1.05 billion deployed in 2015.

Venture firms are also backing medical-technology startups with treatments for atrial fibrillation. They include VytronUS Inc., which disclosed a $49 million "http://www.wsj.com/articles/vytronus-raises-49m-series-c-for-atrial-fibrillation-treatment-1469804486" round in July.

Andreessen Horowitz General Partner Vijay Pande is joining the Cardiogram board as an observer.

Markets
WSJ Pro Conference: Volcker Resists Bank Breakups, Bair Opposes Risk-Weighting; See summaries of interviews with more than a dozen policy makers and experts at our conference in New York

4,307 words
19 October 2016
03:12
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

Mr. Volcker said, "If you break up a $2 trillion bank into $4[00 billion] or $500 billion banks, you've got a problem." An earlier version of this article misquoted him as saying "into four or five banks." He also said the SEC's regulation of broker dealers is in need of reassessment at Tuesday's conference. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated he spoke Monday. (Oct. 18)

NEW YORK More than a dozen policy makers and experts including Paul Volcker and Sheila Bair joined Pro Financial Regulation at a conference Tuesday to discuss the future of financial regulation: what has worked and what hasn't since the financial crisis, the challenges and opportunities presented by fintech innovation, emerging vulnerabilities in the financial system, the road ahead for enforcement, and changes to expect from Washington after the November election.

Read tweets from the event "https://twitter.com/WSJFinReg?ref_src=twsrc^google|twcamp^serp|twgr^author" and check "http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation" in coming days for full videos from the event.

Below are summaries of each interview.

Paul Volcker: The Long View

Chairman of the Volcker Alliance; Federal Reserve chairman from 1979-87

Paul Volcker pushed back against calls to break up big Wall Street banks, and disputed claims that his eponymous Volcker rule had caused a damaging diminution of liquidity in financial markets.

Mr. Volcker, said he understood the appeal of breaking up complex and potentially compromised institutions but is "not convinced" such moves would improve financial stability.

"If you break up a $2 trillion bank into $4[00 billion] or $500 billion banks, you've got a problem," he said. "It's an interesting debate &.But from a stability standpoint it's not on my list."

Mr. Volcker said the Volcker rule isn't an antitrading rule, but a rule against speculation, suggesting claims it had reduced liquidity in some markets were motivated by traders' self-interest.

"Liquidity is in the mind of the beholder &that all depends on the market. A lot of liquidity generates a lot of trading and a lot of risk-taking," he said, suggesting a happy medium is difficult to find. "Go and speculate all you like, but not on my dime."

Mr. Volcker, 89 years old, said claims the rule had imposed $4 billion of compliance costs on banks were "nonsense" and the rule in fact commanded "a lot of support" among bankers themselves, notwithstanding its complexity. "It gets crazy, but that's the way they want it," he said, referring to lobbyists' and lawyers' efforts to complicate a concept proprietary trading that should be obvious to any banker or regulator.

Mr. Volcker weighed in on the Wells Fargo scandal, suggesting it showed banks should separate the roles of chairman and chief executive, a relatively rare practice in the U.S.

"It shouldn't kill the bank. But it is an interesting wake-up call but not just for Wells Fargo, but for some other banks, too," he said. "I'm sure their culture was wonderful on paper."

"Did management have the skill and capacity to control the spirits unleashed by this compensation system? The answer is obviously no," he said.

Last year, Mr. Volcker's Alliance issued a paper calling for a strengthened Financial Stability Oversight Council, to make prudential supervision more rational. On Tuesday, Mr. Volcker said the Securities and Exchange Commission's regulation of broker dealers is in need of particular reassessment. "You hear the most incoherent supervision in that area, which is the heart of the system," he said.

Adam Creighton

Sheila Bair: Postcrisis Did We Get It Right?

President of Washington College; former Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. chief from 2006-11

Sheila Bair recommended junking the whole risk-weighting system underpinning the Basel capital accord, and called on Hillary Clinton to be clearer about her banking proposals.

Ms. Bair said the evidence showed simple leverage ratios are superior to the risk-weighting framework, which is subject to gaming by banks.

"The best solution is to chuck [risk-weighting]," she said, arguing that leaving banks room to estimate their own risk weights would lead to a race to the bottom. "The best banks will have the highest risk weights, so using this framework penalizes good management," she said.

Ms. Bair's comments come amid a spat, especially in Europe, between regulators who want to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/basel-officialsays-some-banksmay-face-capital-requirement-increases-1475870402" to set their own risk weights, and banks that fear simplifying the framework will result in significantly increased capital requirements.

"[The crisis] showed those banks with the highest risk-weighted capital were most likely to get in trouble," she said, advocating a higher leverage ratio than the 3% minimum Basel III has mandated.

Ms. Bair also took aim at the liquidity coverage ratio, part of the Basel framework that requires banks to hold a high share of liquid assets to ensure they can fund.

"The idea you can calibrate what's liquid and what's not, and create an environment where banks might be selling to raise cash &. is flawed," she said.

On next month's election, Ms. Bair said there was a "a lot of uncertainty" with the Democratic Party platform, which vaguely endorses a "21st century Glass Steagall Act," as it related to financial reform.

The Clinton team "has Gary Gensler, who I have a lot of confidence in," she said, referring to the former head of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission. She also urged Mrs. Clinton to be more specific about her regulatory plans. "I would feel better about it if she came out with something definitive," Ms. Bair said. "Does she want to increase capital?"

She expressed concern that the Clintons had a "Wall Street centric view of the world," adding that she thinks "you need greater viewpoints. I worry she won't be drawing from a broad enough range of [perspectives]."

Adam Creighton

Sarah Bloom Raskin: Can Government Ensure Cybersecurity?

Deputy Treasury secretary

Cyberthreats to financial institutions have transformed into a global problem that requires united efforts from government and business, according to Sarah Bloom Raskin.

While government cyberprotection efforts have become more rigorous and proactive, rather than reactive, in recent years, partnering with the private sector is essential to their success, Ms. Raskin said.

"It's something that the government can't do alone," she said.

In recent years, the episodic attacks on a bank's security perimeter have been replaced by regular attempts at stealing the financial institution's "crown jewels," Ms. Raskin said.

"The goals have changed &it's not so much nuisance, but misappropriation," she said.

The Treasury has collaborated with financial institutions to set up information-sharing mechanisms and identify the vectors driving the attacks, she said. An attacker frequently uses the same technology, methodology and internet addresses to target a series of institutions.

"We would like, in real time, for those vectors to be known across the financial sector," Ms. Raskin said.

The Treasury also is working with the Group of Seven leading industrialized nations on a cybersecurity framework.

"We now have realized that in order to get this right, we have to think about this globally," Ms. Raskin said.

Tatyana Shumsky

Mark Warner: The Congressional Agenda

Democratic U.S. senator from Virginia

Sen. Mark Warner said he is "losing patience" with bank regulators over their disagreements on what some large banks need to do to plan for an orderly unwinding that would avoid the need for taxpayer money.

Mr. Warner helped write the legislation requiring banks to submit "living wills" and devise a resolution process for how they would go out of business in the event of a failure. But the regulators reviewing those plans the Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. have disagreed with each other on the plans of two banks whose efforts were considered inadequate earlier this year. Mr. Warner said that six years after Dodd-Frank was enacted, it is time for the regulators to take stronger action in tandem.

If there is "another year of that kind of disagreement without some kind of commonality, then I think you would see even someone like me, who is one of the co-fathers, that would say we need a new threshold," Mr. Warner said. "My fix would have been a great deal of both public and private communication with those institutions."

Mr. Warner said he also is losing patience with regulators who haven't taken direct action against individuals. He cited the settlement with Wells Fargo & Co., which acknowledged creating nearly two million unauthorized accounts. The regulators have indicated in public testimony that individuals could be cited, but the $185 million in fines against the bank came as part of a settlement in which the company neither confirmed nor denied wrongdoing. That, Mr. Warner said, is frustrating.

"I was introduced as the moderate on regulatory issues, and I'm at that kind of breaking point, too, of frustration where we see these kinds of actions and yet no institution or individual ever seems to be held accountable," he said.

Rachel Witkowski

J. Christopher Giancarlo: Regulating Markets

Republican commissioner at the Commodity Futures Trading Commission

J. Christopher Giancarlo likens policing markets to high-speed highways policed by troopers in 1970s-model cruisers.

"I wish we were more equipped," he said, citing the stark example of CFTC regulations that still refer to trading pits when most trades are now done though virtual exchanges.

"We spend so much time looking backwards to the last crisis that we don't spend enough time looking forward to the next crisis," he said.

Swap-data repositories, a requirement following the 2008 financial crisis to shed light on the complex web of trades, have fallen short, Mr. Giancarlo said.

Mr. Giancarlo, adopting a "21st century markets need 21st century regulation" mantra, says U.S. regulators need to look to the 1990s effort to regulate the internet, and sees promise in blockchain technology and its real-time digital ledgers.

"Imagine if we had access to the blockchain," said Mr. Giancarlo, who favors the U.K. approach of setting up a regulatory "sandbox" that lets companies test ideas without being bogged down by the authorization process.

Cryptocurrencies digital currencies based on cryptography need clarification from Congress, Mr. Giancarlo said.

Also of concern, Mr. Giancarlo said, is a proposal that would give the CFTC unfettered access to high-frequency trading firms' source code the secret sauce behind their trading moves.

The proposal, known as Regulation AT, "is a first step to move into this 21st century digital role," Mr. Giancarlo said, but it would strip away the right to challenge a search and could turn into a "slippery slope," opening the door for the "alphabet soup of government agencies" to access the critical information.

He said he fears that information could end up being stored in some government warehouse without adequate security, just like the Ark of the Covenant in "Raiders of the Lost Ark."

Maria Armental

Barney Frank: If Hillary Clinton Wins the Election

Democratic U.S. representative from Massachusetts from 1981-2012; chairman of the House Financial Services Committee from 2007-11

Barney Frank said a Treasury secretary with financial experience would be a good idea, avoiding any recommendations but providing two suggestions on how to improve the financial overhaul law that bears his name.

The Massachusetts Democrat said the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act needs tweaking but argued the law has "already worked well and will stand the test of time."

Still, he said, the $50 billion asset threshold that triggers Fed supervision should be doubled, at the least, and small banks with assets below $10 billion should be exempted from complying with the Volcker rule.

"We have a problem with small banks: I think part of the problem some of them are being over-lawyered going through too much effort to show" they comply with the law, he said.

Mr. Frank also said "risk retention," where lenders are required to remain exposed to at least part of the value of loans they had made, was crucial to maintaining market discipline.

"The single biggest problem we have was when lending switched from people borrowing money from a person they had to pay back to [a system of] securitization," he said, bemoaning a political deal that had exempted residential mortgages from risk retention.

Asked about the next Treasury secretary, Mr. Frank said "someone with financial services experience and knowledge is a good idea," noting President George W. Bush had churned through three Treasury secretaries, two of whom had relatively little financial experience. He also said the "toughest two regulators" in his time in Congress were Gary Gensler, who had been chairman of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission, and Sheila Bair, who had headed the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.

Mr. Frank, now on the board of Signature Bank, also said the Dodd-Frank Act, which runs to thousands of pages, was less complex than people thought because it rolled into one law what "had been done in eight or nine" in the past, citing the post-depression era Glass-Steagall and Investment Company acts.

Adam Creighton

Panel Discussion: Policing Wall Street

Jamal El-Hindi, acting director of the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network

Robert Werner, head of financial crime compliance at HSBC Holdings

M. Kendall Day, chief of the asset forfeiture and money-laundering section, criminal division, at the Justice Department

A top government financial-crimes enforcement official said he wanted to strike a fair balance between ensuring banks comply with new money-laundering controls, while taking care to avoid disruptions in the payments chain.

Jamal El-Hindi, acting director of FinCEN, the U.S. Treasury unit that collects suspicious activity reports, said that the goal wasn't wholesale derisking, but a route that improves banks' behavior and transparency without them pulling back.

"We do have to realize we have never told them their goal is to keep everything out," said Mr. El-Hindi. "It's not a zero-tolerance regime; we're not asking for perfection."

Robert Werner said on the same panel that the issue is where to draw the line between making banks resilient, while preventing them from exiting whole business segments out of fear of falling foul of rules.

"It's not zero [tolerance], but where is [the line]?" he asked. "Banks have been left to set the risk appetite around that."

Mr. Werner warned that moves by banks to derisk in response to money-laundering regulations could change the payments system in ways that are unpredictable. He said banks are being hyperconservative in response to the controls, to stay on the right side of the law.

"Banks' risk appetite for providing those services to other banks is inevitably shrinking around what kind of monitoring and testing we will do," he said. "There is going to be a tension between management of risk in the institution &versus policy makers who want to have a robust international payments system."

Mr. Werner said the risk was the resiliency of the correspondent banking system. He suggested banks focus on more limited services, versus an all-or-nothing approach, and tailoring services to their risks.

"Any kind of adjustment where business feels comfortable &we would feel supportive," said Mr. El-Hindi.

Also on the panel, Kendall Day said the Justice Department is reviewing whether to bring civil cases against individuals over money-laundering violations, in an apparent departure from its typical focus on large institutions.

Mr. Day said the agency is increasingly looking to deploy its resources toward pursuing individual civil cases. He said historically the agency has focused on larger institutions, which can generate large fines and settlements, but there are additional opportunities the Justice Department sees that aren't maximum recovery cases.

"In the past, my colleagues would bring [cases] where there were the deepest pockets to bring a suit," Mr. Day said. A new memorandum at the agency makes clear that Justice Department civil colleagues won't now be solely focused on maximizing recovery, he added.

Previously, the Justice Department has investigated individuals in the context of anti-money-laundering violations at smaller banks, mostly where suspicious activity is easier to locate to a defined group of people.

Compliance officers are usually the "easiest person you can make a case against," said Mr. Werner, a former director of FinCEN.

But Mr. Day said the DOJ's new line of attack won't focus exclusively on bank compliance officers.

Katy Burne

Lawrence Kudlow: If Donald Trump Wins the Election

Senior contributor to CNBC; informal adviser to Republican presidential nominee Donald Trump

Lawrence Kudlow has signaled that the controversial Consumer Financial Protection Bureau would be overhauled should Mr. Trump be elected president.

"I don't know what it's doing inside the Fed, never seen anything like it. I'm not sure the Fed would like it there," he said, suggesting the current governance gave the CFPB director "way too much power." He said the CFPB's constitutionality was in question a federal appeals court last week declared the agency's unusual independence to be unconstitutional and said he preferred a board of two Democrats and two Republicans from which the president would choose the director.

Mr. Kudlow also scolded the Republican Party for what he called an insufficient focus on growth, citing the past 16 years, including the eight years of George W. Bush's presidency.

"Growth is a job creator and wage increaser, helps solve poverty and long-term debt problems," the CNBC senior contributor said. "I think Republicans are more on the growth path, but not necessarily to my satisfaction," he added, noting growth had averaged 2% a year since 2000 compared with 3.5% over the 16 years before that.

"I'm a John F. Kennedy Democrat and a Reagan Republican," he said. "They were both for growth."

Asked about who would make a good Treasury secretary, Mr. Kudlow declined to offer suggestions but said former Fed governor Kevin Warsh "a young man with tremendous financial ability" and Stanford economics professor John Taylor "an astute monetary analyst" were good people on the Republican side of politics.

Adam Creighton

Antonio Weiss: The Obama Record

Counselor to Treasury Secretary Jacob Lew

Antonio Weiss has staunchly defended the Obama administration's financial reform record, but singled out Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and the market for U.S. Treasurys as unfinished business.

Mr. Weiss said President Barack Obama had overseen the "greatest degree of financial reform in 75 years" that had tilted the financial sector back to supporting the real economy.

"In January and February this year, oil and share prices were collapsing. August of last year, equities collapsed in an unexpected collapse: You did not hear that the fin system was at risk. You didn't here fin institutions were on the verge of failure," he said, pointing to the extra $750 billion of capital U.S. banks had amassed since the financial crisis.

He said "a lot" of things were still on the agenda and warned Congress the administration would remain a steadfast opponent of "riders" on omnibus bills designed to unpick past reforms.

"The public and taxpayers would be served by comprehensive housing finance reform," he said, noting that state-backed Fannie and Freddie had been in conservatorship for eight years.

"Right now, we have a situation where the taxpayer is on the hook for $187 billion that were injected at conservatorship, and in the taxpayer backstop to [government-sponsored enterprises].

"Tens of millions of Americans do not have access to appropriate housing."

Mr. Weiss also highlighted changes in the $13 trillion market for U.S. Treasurys, recently subject to the first review since 1988, prompted by the increased role of algorithmic trading, hedge funds and nonbank traders.

"We've moved from the secondary market carried out by primary dealers where there's a whole array of players. We hope that [the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority] will soon finalize a rule which will provide for official collection of all these transactions," he said.

Adam Creighton

Panel Discussion: Governing Fintech

Jo Ann Barefoot, senior fellow at Harvard University's John F. Kennedy School of Government and former deputy Comptroller of the Currency

John Beccia, general counsel and chief compliance officer of Circle

Hans Morris, managing partner of Nyca Partners

Upstart nonbank financial services firms are hampered by having to register with individual states to make loans or transfer money, according to a panel of fintech executives.

State-by-state regulation is "painful and expensive, and doesn't serve a public purpose," said venture capital investor and LendingClub Corp. Chairman Hans Morris. Many upstart financial technology firms, such as LendingClub, have opted not to become banks, and so must either partner with a national bank to make loans nationally or go state by state to register as a money transmitter or lender.

That process is expensive and difficult, requiring steps such as fingerprinting of executives in each state, said Mr. Morris, who is also managing partner at Nyca Partners, a venture-capital firm that was an early investor in LendingClub.

"I can't think of one person who would stand up and defend state-by-state processing," he said. "It's quite unbelievable, and it's not good regulation. None of these states are really regulating activities."

John Beccia of Circle, which offers a mobile app to make person-to-person payments using blockchain technology, said the difficulty is compounded by the fact that many banks don't want to partner with fintech firms.

The patchwork of state regulation is "a barrier to entry," he said. "Banks are under a lot of pressure post-Dodd-Frank, and many don't want to work with fintech companies."

Circle was the first firm to apply for a bitcoin license in New York, one of the first states to create a regime to regulate the digital currency.

The complexity of state regulation is a major reason that the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency is evaluating a limited banking charter that could be used by fintech firms that wouldn't be quite as onerous as a traditional license, said former banking regulator Jo Ann Barefoot.

"State regulators are working on trying to add more streamlining to address this problem, an incredibly serious problem," said Ms. Barefoot. "That's one reason they're looking at a national charter."

For now, she said, fintech firms have to live in a "permanent state of regulator uncertainty."

"Regulators are going to need a change of philosophy and culture," said Mr. Beccia. "We have to encourage innovation in a sandbox environment, and we have to encourage banks to encourage the derisking of fintech firms." A regulatory "sandbox" lets companies test new technologies and ideas without requirements to get full authorization.

Mr. Morris said an alternative limited charter would be "a major policy benefit" to the U.S.

Telis Demos

Panel Discussion: Finreg meets AI

Eugene Ludwig, founder and chief executive of Promontory Financial Group

Alistair Rennie, general manager of solutions at IBM Watson Analytics

Former national bank regulator Eugene Ludwig is a firm believer that artificial intelligence holds the promise of helping to stave off regulatory and consumer risks.

He believes in it so much that Mr. Ludwig is selling his prominent regulatory consultant firm, Promontory Financial Group in Washington, D.C., to International Business Machines Corp. with the plan to build an AI-based consultancy called Watson Financial Services. IBM's Watson technology platform uses natural-language processing and self-learning to sift through mounds of data. Mr. Ludwig said it would help firms predict potential risk areas as well as consumer behavior a way that many regulators and other firms are studying to help prevent systemic risks and financial fraud.

The challenge is to "solve the problems in a more effective way and a more efficient way," Mr. Ludwig said. "Watson is a huge giant leap forward. It's not just like a few steps."

"The typical technological approach has been to &build a rules-based system that is often complex and often very much like a black box," said Alistair Rennie of IBM Watson Analytics. "We just don't think that's viable going forward because of the time to react to a change, to react to a market condition is just too slow."

IBM announced the acquisition of Promontory in September. The expansion of Watson in the financial services industry is under way, executives said.

Rachel Witkowski

Markets
Transcript: Sen. Mark Warner at WSJ Pro Financial Regulation Event; The full transcript of WSJ Pro's interview with Sen. Mark Warner (D., Va.) at the Future of Financial Regulation Conference.

3,507 words
19 October 2016
01:28
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Sen. Mark Warner (D., Va.)

Interviewer: Jacob M. Schlesinger, Editor, Financial Regulation, The Wall Street Journal

Location: Four Season Hotel, New York City, New York

Time: 2:25 p.m. EDT

Date: Tuesday, October 18, 2016

Transcript By Superior Transcriptions LLC "http://www.superiortranscriptions.com"

JACOB M. SCHLESINGER: We are privileged to have with us here today through a number of these panels many people who've had a hand in crafting the current regulatory regime that was enacted after the crisis. And the portion of the edifice that was designed in part by Senator Warner was dealing with resolution, Titles I and II of Dodd-Frank to the cognoscenti, the living will process whereby banks  

SENATOR MARK WARNER (D-VA): You got to give Corker part of the credit.

MR. SCHLESINGER: Well, we'll get to that. We'll get to that. Anyway, he's not here.

SEN. WARNER: All right.

MR. SCHLESINGER: But the process where banks have to write credible plans for how they can die without asking taxpayers to pay for the funeral, or something like that.

So, as the proud father or one of the proud fathers of that provision, how do you think it's going so far?

SEN. WARNER: Well, let's roll back the clock a little bit. Obviously, there was major debate at that moment in time between many on my side of the aisle, who were interested in simply breaking up, looking at an asset cap. To me, at that moment, in terms of American competitiveness with other large institutions around the world, that didn't seem to be the appropriate process.

So this notion of living wills, a resolution process, it was obviously new. So I think the jury is still out. I have been frustrated a bit that it has taken as long to get the process kind of worked through. More recently I've been very frustrated with the notion that the Fed and the FDIC can't seem to get on the same page, so we can't really see if it's working or not.

Now, I get the fact that these are complex, large institutions. I think we've made progress on capital standards, liquidity ratios, other issues. You know, there's questions about   in light of the Wells Fargo, you know, so not only too big to fail, but too big to manage, which I think are legitimate questions not addressed by Section 165. I think we will only see 165, its effectiveness, if we can make sure that we see the regulators get on the same page in terms of their standards   I'm losing patience with that failure to get on the same page   and then to see, if an institution falls in a non-performing category, what are some of the remedies going to be.

Now, obviously this far after Dodd-Frank, just to say it's still too early to tell is not a fully satisfactory answer. I'm the first to acknowledge that. But I think  

MR. SCHLESINGER: The famous quote   was it Zhou Enlai, when he was asked what he thought of the French Revolution, said it's too soon to tell?

SEN. WARNER: Too soon to tell.

MR. SCHLESINGER: So he's got another hundred years before we can  

SEN. WARNER: I don't get a hundred years. But I think that, you know, we've seen two years in a row   well, we've seen multiple years in a row now where there's been great disparity between the Fed and the FDIC. The last year it's gotten down to the two institutions. If we had another year of that kind of disagreement without some commonality, then I think you would see even someone like me, who is one of the co-fathers, that would say, hey, we need to take a fresh look at this because it's not working.

MR. SCHLESINGER: So what are the alternatives? Let's assume that your frustration continues and it goes beyond the letter that you wrote saying  

SEN. WARNER: The thing   the thing that I would hope would be that   I don't have intimate knowledge of the workings of all these institutions, but from just a   from a rational standpoint, seeing how difficult it's been to do resolution, and when we think about the potential overall international overhang with Deutsche Bank right now, I think the idea that any of these institutions could be simply resolved even with a single point of entry is probably not fully believable to me. So what I would like to see in a certain sense is that if there was an institution that fell outside of that category and there was unanimity of opinion, to see, OK, what would the regulators then do in terms of corrective action. Then I think we'd know is this a tool that is going to make sure that we have   we have put in place a regulatory regime in advance of a crisis that could potentially foresee that crisis and take prospective action rather than retrospective action.

MR. SCHLESINGER: But in terms of the process of getting the Fed and FDIC on the same page, which   you say you're losing patience with that. Can you fix the living wills process? And what would your fix be?

SEN. WARNER: Well, my fix has been a great deal of both public and private communication with those institutions saying you got to get on the same page or this needs to be, you know, reexamined at a   at a committee level.

MR. SCHLESINGER: You mentioned too big to manage, and you alluded to Wells Fargo. Can you talk a little bit about your views on the Wells Fargo scandal? And what particular remedies do you think, legislative or otherwise, are warranted by that?

SEN. WARNER: Well, listen, I've been familiar with the Wells Fargo team for some time. I was, candidly, as surprised as most, I think. This was the entity that had a less complex set of products. It seemed to be kind of more plain vanilla.

You know, I'm outraged. I think I was very disappointed in the former CEO and chairman's testimony when he repeatedly said that's the responsibility of the board when he is the chairman of the board, did not fly and pass the smell test. And you know, I think it continues to raise the question that when we see this kind of excess and how this can be   this is not explained away when literally millions of Americans' accounts were messed with, misappropriated to some level or another. And simply saying we're going to do settlements where there's not acknowledgement of guilt I think is not a satisfactory answer.

So I think the jury is still out on what, you know, Justice Department or other enforcement agencies may take on or not take on. But the   I think the American public wants to see   and I hear this on a repeated basis   that if there are these kind of actions taking place, someone pays the price rather than simply a lump sum paid with no acknowledgement of guilt.

MR. SCHLESINGER: So you're pushing a hot button there that goes beyond the Wells Fargo settlement, which is, as you say, a lot of these settlements involve sort of no admission of guilt, paying a big fine, and that's it. Do you feel as if, again, legislatively, that there might be some need for  

SEN. WARNER: My hope would be that we would not get to that legislative piece. But if we never see action   and I   and listen, I appreciate we had the CFTC up here earlier. We've had, you know, the SEC. You know, the irony is that some of the folks that I work with are on a constant effort to cut their budgets, so their ability to follow through on an enforcement action and take it all the way through a legal process is somewhat constrained by their resources. So, you know, I have some empathy for that. But at the same time, you know, to continue to say, when excess after excess   and remember how I was introduced. I was introduced as the moderate on regulatory issues, and I'm, though, at that kind of breaking point, too, of frustration, where we see these kind of actions and yet no institution or individual ever seem to be held accountable. And that will build up, and that is playing out in the political process right now.

Today you're going to see, later, folks come up from the   what happens if the Democrats or what happens if, God forbid, on the other side. (Laughter.) But you know, when there is agreement   and I say this as somebody who came up through the financial industry and was blessed to do really well   I believe that when both sides are saying the system is rigged and we continue to see these excesses and no one held accountable, that is   you know, I'd like to take you around my state and try to make the argument why that's OK.

MR. SCHLESINGER: So let's talk about the election. And let's assume your desired scenario, which is the Democrats win not only the presidency, but the Senate. What is going to be the agenda from the Senate Finance Committee? What would the priorities   the regulatory priorities be to you?

SEN. WARNER: Well, let me   let me give you less the Democrat Senate Finance Committee and give you more what Mark Warner's agenda items would be.

Here's one of my concerns. I think there is a   much more recognition amongst most members of Congress that we have rightfully earned our 8 percent approval rating and that we've got to put some points on the board. But my concern as a Democrat is, if we simply   let's assume conventional wisdom: Clinton and Kaine win, Democrats take back the Senate by a narrow margin, the House remains Republican. If we simply come back and say let's rehash four or five pre-litigated issues and expect a different result, with only marginal changes around the edges, we're not going to see anything. And the American people want to see some points put on the board.

So what I would do on a regulatory basis, I would look at, for example, the changing nature of the workforce   the fact that, right now, a third of the American workforce is in some level of contingent work, whether it's gig economy, part time, short-termers. They have no social insurance. So the whole social contract that was created in our country in the 1930s and '40s, that was built upon the presumption that you're going to work for the same firm for 20 years   so we're the only country in the world that says social insurance is dependent upon your labor status. I think we need to move to a portable benefit system. I think we need to have   engage with you in this room on the question of, is something fundamentally altered with capitalism? And as somebody who has benefitted from capitalism, believe in the system, when we've seen the average term of hold on a public stock has gone from eight years to four months; when we've seen corporate profits spent 50 percent reinvested in the business, now to 95 percent spent on share buyback on dividends; when large companies that have huge CAPEX are taking away from that CAPEX, you know, to fight back activist investors. Now, I don't want to say and advocate a Japanese economy kind of simplification. But I do think this notion of the long/short challenge, whether we are moving so   whether technology and other things have accelerated so much that we're so focused on short-term results versus long-term value creation I think is a fundamental issue that a lot of folks in America affects their lives in terms of their jobs, in terms of their futures. And you know, that would be whether it's regulatory, whether it's   it is through other efforts.

I honestly think   you know, and this would be   I hope would be probably not a place where government ought to start, but where you ought to start, whether you are on the financial side or particularly those of you on the corporate side, those of you who are on the asset management side. You know, do we need to think about a fiduciary duty definition that goes more than just short term that includes shareholders and other   or employees and other constituencies? Should we be able to look at more long-term value creation? Should we look at how we compensate everybody in this room more towards three-to five-year results? Obviously there are things we could do as policymakers around the edges   differential capital gains rate based on long-term hold, think it makes a lot of   a lot of sense there.

But, you know, to be provocative, if you do a major share buyback campaign, should management be beneficiaries of that share buyback campaign? Should you look   and this would be easy to say, but impossible to implement   but, you know, if you've got lots of cash on your balance sheet   and yes, we got to do international tax reform and do repatriation   but if you're out borrowing money simply for share buyback purposes, should we look at that deductibility of that interest? Should we say, maybe if there's going to be this pushup in value, some of that pushup in value needs to go to the employees as well? We need to make sure   please understand that the anger and frustration in America is so visceral and real, and it's so immediate, and we've not even hit artificial intelligence, automation that's coming in terms of automated vehicles. This wave is going to sweep us all out if we don't come to grips on a way that I think we can make capitalism work for everybody again, whether that's a capitalism 2.0 or 21st-century capitalism. But those would be some of the things I'd be asking.

MR. SCHLESINGER: Does anyone in the audience have questions? We have time for a few. This gentleman right over here.

SEN. WARNER: And I'm happy to go back to financial reform. You can put me back in my box, so  

Q: (Off mic)   and has not been dealt with by Dodd-Frank, and that is the GSEs.

SEN. WARNER: Thank you, thank you, thank you. As somebody who spent two-and-a-half to three years learning more about housing finance than I ever wanted to know, number one; and, number two, when I got into the plumbing, it's remarkable to me that this system worked as well as it did for as long as it did before it blew up. And the notion of, you know, private-sector gain and public-sector accepting the losses just is not palatable.

Now, the effort that, again, Bob Corker and I and then Tim Johnson and Mike Crapo put together was   it was, obviously, maybe a bit too intricate, and the transition time was a challenge. But I think housing finance reform is absolutely the undone part of reform that needs to be done. I think our frame of some explicit guarantee, but only as kind of last party for the federal government to play, with more private capital in front, was still some insurance that low- and moderate-income people and first-time homebuyers are served. I think the notional idea of the 10 basis points, let's say, as a   as a set aside that would go towards that duty-to-serve type obligations would be remarkably helpful to folks struggling to get into the housing market.

I'm pleased to see that both Gene Sterling and Mark Zandi have put out kind of a new frame, and there's one more kind of on the center-right that Michael Bright and some others are working on right now. I think within that frame there really can be a chance to move on that. That is definitely an item that has   that has been left.

SEN. WARNER: Sure.

SEN. WARNER: I have not gone through with explicit detail every piece of  

MR. SCHLESINGER: Well, let me ask you about a couple of things that maybe are a little more controversial. She's open to some form of financial transactions tax, which has gotten a lot of traction in the Democratic Party. How do you feel about that?

SEN. WARNER: I'm open to that discussion. You know, we have a financial transaction tax right now. It's a tiny, tiny amount, a faction of a cent that is used to support the exchanges. Now, the question is how far up you go before you push business offshore. And you've seen other countries that have not   that have talked about it that have found hard parts in terms of the implementation. It is easier talked about than implemented. But the notion that this is a   couldn't even be discussed or, you know, would completely explode the markets, you know, or you couldn't ever put it in place, is just not factually accurate, since   again, I should have it in my head, but you know, it's   Gene Ludwig would have this number   but there is a tiny financial transaction tax right now that takes place on every share transaction.

MR. SCHLESINGER: So one of the issues   you mentioned Senator Warren, who's also on the Finance Committee, and one of the issues   the big powers that your committee will have, even if the House still   Republicans still hold the House, is confirming appointees. Senator Warren has made no secret of her view that personnel is policy, as she puts it. SEN. WARNER: Senator Warren and I actually work together on a whole lot of issues. Couldn't get to the final stage, but on things like the Jumpstart Reform we're there.

He was a Chase banker before he became a regulator. Gary Gensler probably made most of you in the room mad at one point or another; he was a Goldman Sachs partner.

So should we make sure that the regulators are balanced and don't simply come from industry? Absolutely. But to take one broad brush and say, per se, anyone that actually has worked in the intricacies of an industry that is extraordinarily complex can't serve the public I don't agree with.

MR. SCHLESINGER: You seem open to a lot of things that, as you say, we might be surprised a moderate is   might not be open to. Both party platforms, surprisingly, embrace the restoration of Glass-Steagall. How do you feel about that?

SEN. WARNER: You know, I don't think   I don't know how you put that genie back in the bottle, and I don't know how you would do that on an American-only basis.

NewSpring Collects $253.8 Million for Latest Growth Capital Fund

By Chris Cumming

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

NewSpring Capital is closing in on its target for the firm's fourth technology-focused growth-equity fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission.

The firm aims to raise $300 million for NewSpring Growth Capital IV LP, which has gathered at least $253.8 million so far, according to the filing.

NewSpring has been raising the fund since last year and made its first sale in October 2015, according to the filing.

The fund size is a step up from the predecessor vehicle, which closed at $250 million in 2013.

The Radnor, Pa., firm didn't respond to a request for comment.

NewSpring Growth Capital invests in growth-stage business services and technology, including infrastructure software, data analytics and online commerce. The firm targets companies in the mid-Atlantic U.S. that generate annual revenue of $5 million and $50 million.

Along with its growth-equity funds, NewSpring also has health-care, mezzanine debt and lower midmarket funds. The firm last month "http://www.wsj.com/articles/newspring-sells-three-portfolio-companies-1474916680".

Pro Private Markets
Chorus Uses AI to Analyze Sales Calls

By Tomio Geron 
470 words
18 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

Chorus raised $6.3 million in seed funding. A previous version of this story incorrectly said it raised $6.3 million in Series A funding. (Oct. 19, 2016)

Technology companies have been looking for novel ways to apply artificial intelligence technology to industries. Startup Chorus aims to arm salespeople with AI, promising sales pitch refinements.

San Francisco- and Tel Aviv-based Chorus has raised $6.3 million in seed financing led by Emergence Capital Partners.

Chorus isn't alone. A crowded arena of startups want to improve sales, such as InsideSales and ClearSlide. But Chorus analyzes the actual sales conversations to understand the elements of winning deals.

Cloud computing has helped upend traditional enterprise software markets, but artificial intelligence offers a next wave of industry change.

"How machine learning and AI will interact with enterprise software will be as fundamental a sea change as the move from client-server to the cloud was," said Gordon Ritter, a co-founder and general partner at Emergence Capital. Mr. Ritter invested early in Salesforce.com.

Chorus, which integrates with Salesforce.com and webconferencing tools, records sales calls and then uses speech recognition and natural language processing to extract information from the voice data. The software identifies topics, even if they were not mentioned. For example, mentions of Hillary Clinton and Donald Trump would be highlighted as "politics."

Chorus's dashboard shows whether planned topics were covered in the conversation or not, such as "budget," or "follow up." It also shows via a color-coded chart who was speaking when, to identify, for example, if a sales person was speaking too much.

Salespeople can also identify when a competitor's name came up, and what was said to win that deal versus what was said to lose the deal.

Looking at this aggregated data, sales reps and managers can see what techniques, topics or approaches work best for winning deals.

This granular data is typically not captured without voice data, since only about 5% of a conversation is written into notes by salespeople, said Roy Raanani, Chorus chief executive and co-founder.

The technology can identify such things as: "high engagement" with a prospect, an unprofessional speaking manner, hesitations in speech, or speech that is too slow or too fast. It can also identify voice pitch, and how much pitch harmony there is between a salesperson and prospect.

For example, a quick change in pitch can indicate engagement, said Chorus co-founder Micha Breakstone. The software does not currently make recommendations on this type of data but could in the future.

Chorus customers include Cisco's Meraki, Marketo, Qualtrics and Talkable.

"Chorus helps us understand, dissect and ultimately distribute best practices to get new sales representatives up to speed faster while improving existing sales representatives' win rates," said Charlie Besecker, head of global enablement at Qualtrics, in an email.

Write to Tomio Geron at "mailto:tomio.geron@wsj.com"

Markets
Kuwait Firm Expands With GE Office Deal in Ohio; Cincinnati property purchase shows how foreign investors play bigger role in U.S. commercial real estate, even in smaller markets

By Peter Grant 
783 words
18 October 2016
15:52
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A Kuwait-based financial-services firm seeking more diversity in a world awash in cheap oil is paying $107 million for a new Cincinnati office building occupied by General Electric Co. in its biggest real-estate investment in the U.S.

KAMCO Investment Co.'s purchase of the new development, named General Electric @ The Banks, is a sign that a new cadre of foreign investors is playing a bigger role in U.S. commercial real estate, even in smaller markets like Cincinnati.

The deal comes at a tricky time for U.S. commercial real estate. Overall U.S. sales volume was down 12.2% from last year to $299.1 billion as of the end of August, according to data firm Real Capital Analytics.

But a few foreign players are boosting spending. Chinese investors have purchased or have contracts to buy $12.6 billion worth of property in 2016, more than double last year, while investment from Kuwait jumped to $726 million from $500 million, Real Capital said.

What's more, KAMCO's investment in Cincinnati comes as foreign and other investors increasingly are looking to smaller cities for deals with higher yields.

Foreign investment used to focus on major markets such as New York, San Francisco and Chicago. But prices in those cities have soared in recent years, driving down yields or so-called capitalization rates, a common metric in the real-estate industry that shows the relationship between price and income.

At this later stage in the cycle, cap rates are more attractive for buyers in smaller markets. For example, the cap rate in KAMCO's purchase of the 12-story Cincinnati building is about 5.5%, according to people involved in the deal, meaning that the building's annual income is about 5.5% of its purchase price. In cities like New York and San Francisco cap rates of high quality office buildings are well below 5%.

KAMCO, which is listed on the Dubai stock exchange, has been expanding its investments geographically as it deals with a "dramatic drop in oil prices," the company said in its 2015 annual report. The Cincinnati purchase is part of that diversification effort, according to Faisal Sarkhou, KAMCO's chief executive.

KAMCO is looking for other U.S. property, Mr. Sarkhou said in an interview with The Wall Street Journal. Besides diversification, KAMCO is getting know-how from the U.S. real-estate industry, he said. KAMCO hopes to "cross fertilize" its experience with other participants in U.S. deals "to build at home and develop new markets," he said.

Participants in the Cincinnati deal include 90 North Real Estate Partners LP, an investment company with expertise in working with Middle East partners. The London-based firm helped negotiate and structure the purchase of the building from its developer, Atlanta-based Carter & Associates LLC, and also took a small equity stake.

About 60% of the purchase price came from Des Moines-based Principal Global Investors, according to Daniel Cooper, who is head of 90 North's operations in North America. Principal's investment was structured as a lease to keep the deal compliant with the financial rules of Islamic or "Shariah" law, he said. Principal didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

KAMCO's risk is limited because General Electric has leased most of the space in the building for 15 years, with options for another 25 years. GE, which has links to Cincinnati dating back to 1948, occupied the building last month.

GE two years ago leased the space on the banks of the Ohio River and promised to move up to 2,000 workers there. With many of the jobs coming from suburban locations, the deal has been hailed as a sign that rust belt cities were beginning to enjoy the same trends that have been rejuvenating larger cities on the East and West Coasts.

Office tenants in Cincinnati occupied an additional 160,000 square feet as of the end of the second quarter, according to real-estate investment-services company JLL. That helped push the vacancy rate down to 15.9%, JLL said.

Downtown Cincinnati also has seen an increase in residential development and the opening of new bars and restaurants. A new $120 million waterfront park has been opening in phases for about five years.

Eastdil Secured LLC represented Carter in the sale to KAMCO.

Everyone's Worst Investing Fears

By Andy Kessler 
907 words
18 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
A11
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Snapchat is not about to disappear -- this newspaper reported earlier this month that the firm may go public next year with a $25 billion valuation. Don't you wish you had invested at a $500 million value? Lots of investors underwater this year sure do.

Last month Perry Capital, which peaked with $15 billion under management in 2007, closed its flagship fund. Richard Perry, a Robert Rubin protege, complained in a recent letter to investors that "this market environment has not worked well for us." Really? The Dow is now within a hair of its all-time high.

Some 10,000 hedge funds invest almost $3 trillion. More funds closed than opened over the past year. With $20 billion in assets, Lansdowne Partners is down almost 15% this year. The Rhode Island State Investment Commission is cutting hedge-fund holdings by half. The California Public Employees' Retirement System droped hedge funds altogether last year. What's going on? This isn't a nasty bear market.

So many asset classes are in a funk. Macro investing, making bets based on major geopolitical trends, was all the rage over the past 25 years. But George Soros breaking the Bank of England is a thing of the past. Debt is now monetized rather than rationalized. There is a glut of commodities -- from forests to food. Energy is fracked. Private equity probably peaked a few years ago, but it hasn't yet marked to market. And bonds? When you have to pay Germany to own its sovereign bonds, it is tough to make money. That leaves stocks.

Equity investors have loved lower interest rates, because they make stocks more attractive than bonds and increase the value of future earnings. But at zero and even negative interest rates, it's a mess. Think of zero rates as a compass that can't point north and only spins around. Dividend-discount models to value stocks are driven by a discount rate that at zero makes every stock worth infinity. So stocks look cheap, even though they've never been more expensive.

What to do? The first rule of investing is that there are no rules. Investing is like fashion. What's hot and what's not changes at the whim of the market. It used to be every fund owned Apple, until it stopped working. Right now we are in what CNBC's Jim Cramer calls a Fang market, because of the power of Facebook, Amazon, Netflix and Google. Google has done the worst of the bunch since January 2015 -- up only 50%. Netflix and Amazon have both doubled. In an economy with 2% GDP growth, not much else works.

Tomorrow's fashion? If I knew for sure, I'd be on a Bora Bora beach. But I can point to the right neighborhood. It's the New over the Old. New productive companies destroying the old ways of doing business.

The market does the dirty work, providing access to capital for those it thinks will be the next wave of great companies. It starves those in decline. This is why BlackBerry doesn't make phones and why General Motors is throwing good money after bad into technology and partners for autonomous vehicles. SoFi is hurting banks.

But even being the New can be fleeting. Apple's iTunes stung music companies by offering reasonably priced songs instead of full albums. And now streaming companies like Spotify offer unlimited music for $10 a month. The Newer destroying the New. I call this Lily Pad investing: You've got to hop from one company to another before the first one drowns. But it's hard.

Finding the next wave of hot companies can be a pain in the assets. The minor leagues for this crop of the New is often venture capital. The New firms are then swallowed up by public investors during initial public offerings. Yet companies are staying private much longer. There is no public market for SoFi or Spotify. Thanks to "financial reform" acts like Sarbanes-Oxley and Dodd-Frank, the market for IPOs has been a ghost town.

Trillions in assets are itching to invest in these markets, but for now investors are stuck with firms that are under attack. Try to find investment in virtual reality, genome editing, drones, voice-control interfaces, artificial intelligence, chatbots or robots. Even immunotherapy and gene-therapy investments are far and few between. These companies become billion-dollar unicorns because they stay private. And not all of them will work. Hyperloop is aptly named.

Just recently, the IPO window has cracked open in a small way. Nutanix -- an integrator of servers and storage that hopes to destroy Hewlett-Packard, IBM and Dell EMC -- saw its stock almost triple in the first few days of trading last week. Earlier IPOs would sure be nice.

The king of the New, Uber founder Travis Kalanick, remarked at a German technology conference this summer, "I say we are going to IPO as late as humanly possible. It'll be one day before my employees and significant others come to my office with pitchforks and torches. We will IPO the day before that. Do you get it?" And that tells you why so many are showing such runty returns.

---

Mr. Kessler, a former hedge-fund manager, is the author of "Eat People" (Portfolio, 2011).

FT REPORT - INVESTING IN CENTRAL & EASTERN EUROPE
Bulgaria strives to become tech capital of the Balkans; Innovation

By Kerin Hope 
857 words
17 October 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys CEE1
02
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The nation's history as the former Soviet Union's IT hub has given it a head start, says Kerin Hope

Darin Madzharov, studying for a PhD in computer science in Belgium, was growing impatient with his sister's calls on Skype seeking help with her Sofia high school science course. So he responded with a stream of video tutorials, which her classmates and friends snapped up.

"They became so popular that I gave up my PhD, came back to Sofia and launched an educational website," the 28-year-old says.

His site, Ucha ("learn" in Bulgarian) took off thanks to  ¬30,000 in funding from Launchub, a local accelerator backed by the European Investment Fund (EIF) and the EU's Jeremie tech start-up programme. Two Bulgarian angel investors came up with  ¬200,000 in a further raising and this year Ucha.se is set to make a profit.

"For 20 lev ( ¬10), the price of a meal, you can sign up for a year's worth of lessons covering any national curriculum subject," says Mr Madzharov.

Bulgaria is striving to catch up with central Europe, where economies based on low-cost manufacturing and exports are shifting to innovative and creative industries. A generation of westernised engineers and programmers with a global outlook now underpins the country's emergence as the technology capital of the Balkans.

Computer science and engineering graduates are no longer migrating in droves to the US and Germany. Local companies employ about 40,000 software engineers while the IT sector contributes more than 3 per cent of the country's output, compared with less than 1 per cent four years ago.

"Bulgaria is an interesting hub for IT development with higher-quality [people] and lower costs than elsewhere in Europe," says Asparuh Koev, chief executive of Transmetrics, an award-winning start-up that uses big data and predictive analytics to improve efficiency in the transport industry.

Sofia and Plovdiv, Bulgaria's second city, have become flourishing outsourcing centres , providing jobs for thousands of IT graduates. Some of these go on to develop products that attract the attention of technology watchers and foreign venture capitalists.

Innovation is what sets Bulgarian outsourcing companies apart, according to Dimitar Mantchev, managing director of Programista, a software developer based in Sofia and Plovdiv. "You make customised software and build a client base, then one of your teams develops a new product and you grow much faster."

Bulgarian IT skills were born under communism. Its state-owned electronics companies specialised in computer hardware and basic programming for the Soviet bloc countries. "It's a strength that we've already had two generations of engineers with a strong R&D background," says Daniel Tomov, founder of Eleven, an accelerator fund backed by the EIF and the Jeremie programme.

Until this year, a lack of venture capital had left fast-growing start-ups short of funding. But the situation has now improved because investors are willing to commit more cash, says Lyuben Belov, co-founder of Launchub, which has backed more than 40 start-ups using EU funds.

Mr Belov is setting up Launchub Ventures, a  ¬20m fund with private backers, to provide up to  ¬1.5m of funding for later-stage companies. "We have all the ingredients of a successful ecosystem - mentors, recycled capital, angel investors and investment funds. And there are about 30 companies that are doing really well with 30-50 employees and revenues above  ¬2m."

Neveq Capital Partners, which has pioneered investments in Bulgarian IT companies, has launched a fund supported by the EIF to invest between  ¬300,000 and  ¬3m in local start-ups targeting the global market. Its portfolio has more than a dozen businesses, among them Vayant, a leader in airfare search technology backed by Lufthansa.

"Funding is still going to be a challenge for later-stage companies but international venture capital funds are showing more interest," says Pavel Ezekiev, a partner at Neveq.

Yet some entrepreneurs worry that a looming skills shortage risks undermining Bulgaria's regional IT hub hopes.

"Universities don't have the resources to teach cutting-edge skills so we're going to face a situation where demand outstrips supply," says Roddy Dervishev, founder of Sibiz, which designs high-performance microchips.

A 13-year veteran of Silicon Valley who came back home, Mr Dervishev is one of several leading entrepreneurs committed to sponsoring IT facilities at Bulgarian universities.

Some industry watchers insist the only way for Bulgaria to sustain growth is to source IT skills from outside the EU, from neighbouring Serbia and Macedonia and even Russia and Ukraine.

The country is also attracting businesses to move here. Last year Vlad Ellis moved his company, Speedflow Communications, which provides customised billing software and voice-overinternet-protocol services for international telecoms operators, from Ukraine to Bulgaria. Half his 50-strong workforce moved with him.

Now based in Plovdiv, Mr Ellis says: "The costs are a bit higher but there are talented people here and you don't have political risk."

Bulgaria strives to become tech capital of the Balkans

17 October 2016

Darin Madzharov, studying for a PhD in computer science in Belgium, was growing impatient with his sister s calls on Skype seeking help with her Sofia high school science course. So he responded with a stream of video tutorials, which her classmates and friends snapped up.

 They became so popular that I gave up my PhD, came back to Sofia and launched an educational website,  the 28-year-old says.

His site, "http://ucha.se/" ( learn  in Bulgarian) took off thanks to  ¬30,000 in funding from Launchub, a local accelerator backed by the European Investment Fund (EIF) and the EU s Jeremie tech start-up programme. Two Bulgarian angel investors came up with  ¬200,000 in a further raising and this year Ucha.se is set to make a profit.

 For 70 lev ( ¬35), the price of a meal, you can sign up for a year s worth of lessons covering any national curriculum subject,  says Mr Madzharov.

Bulgaria is striving to catch up with central Europe, where economies based on low-cost manufacturing and exports are shifting to innovative and creative industries. A generation of westernised engineers and programmers with a global outlook now underpins the country s emergence as the technology capital of the Balkans.

Computer science and engineering graduates are no longer migrating in droves to the US and Germany. Local companies employ about 40,000 software engineers while the IT sector contributes more than 3 per cent of the country s output, compared with less than 1 per cent four years ago.

 Bulgaria is an interesting hub for IT development with higher-quality [people] and lower costs than elsewhere in Europe,  says Asparuh Koev, chief executive of Transmetrics, an award-winning start-up that uses big data and predictive analytics to improve efficiency in the transport industry.

Sofia and Plovdiv, Bulgaria s second city, have become flourishing outsourcing centres, providing jobs for thousands of IT graduates. Some of these go on to develop products that attract the attention of technology watchers and foreign venture capitalists.

Innovation is what sets Bulgarian outsourcing companies apart, according to Dimitar Mantchev, managing director of Programista, a software developer based in Sofia and Plovdiv.  You make customised software and build a client base, then one of your teams develops a new product and you grow much faster. 

Bulgarian IT skills were born under communism. Its state-owned electronics companies specialised in computer hardware and basic programming for the Soviet bloc countries.  It s a strength that we ve already had two generations of engineers with a strong R&D background,  says Daniel Tomov, founder of Eleven, an accelerator fund backed by the EIF and the Jeremie programme.

Until this year, a lack of venture capital had left fast-growing start-ups short of funding. But the situation has now improved because investors are willing to commit more cash, says Lyuben Belov, co-founder of Launchub, which has backed more than 40 start-ups using EU funds.

Mr Belov is setting up Launchub Ventures, a  ¬20m fund with private backers, to provide up to  ¬1.5m of funding for later-stage companies.  We have all the ingredients of a successful ecosystem   mentors, recycled capital, angel investors and investment funds. And there are about 30 companies that are doing really well with 30-50 employees and revenues above  ¬2m. 

Neveq Capital Partners, which has pioneered investments in Bulgarian IT companies, has launched a fund supported by the EIF to invest between  ¬300,000 and  ¬3m in local start-ups targeting the global market. Its portfolio has more than a dozen businesses, among them Vayant, a leader in airfare search technology backed by Lufthansa.

 Funding is still going to be a challenge for later-stage companies but international venture capital funds are showing more interest,  says Pavel Ezekiev, a partner at Neveq.

Yet some entrepreneurs worry that a looming skills shortage risks undermining Bulgaria s regional IT hub hopes.

 Universities don t have the resources to teach cutting-edge skills so we re going to face a situation where demand outstrips supply,  says Roddy Dervishev, founder of Sibiz, which designs high-performance microchips.

A 13-year veteran of Silicon Valley who came back home, Mr Dervishev is one of several leading entrepreneurs committed to sponsoring IT facilities at Bulgarian universities.

Half his 50-strong workforce moved with him.

Now based in Plovdiv, Mr Ellis says:  The costs are a bit higher but there are talented people here and you don t have political risk.
