Vista Equity Partners collected at least $8.9 billion in investor commitments for its flagship private-equity fund this summer in a year that technology has become private equity's most popular target.

The Austin, Texas, firm, which could raise about $10 billion for the fund, exceeded the initial $8 billion goal for its largest-ever war chest for software and information technology buyout deals.

Vista Equity Partners Fund VI LP hit the $8.9 billion mark in August, according to an investor memorandum.

The firm's latest buyout fund is expected to focus on companies valued between $400 million and $5 billion. Although the fund will make equity investments of $200 million to $1 billion, Vista has the firepower to do bigger deals and to pool capital with co-investors.

Vista the firm behind big-ticket bets on insurance software maker Solera Holdings Inc. and data analytics company Tibco Software Inc. has been shifting its focus to larger businesses in recent years, according to the investor document.

As the firm looks to edge out rivals, Vista of late also has used more debt in its deals, according to the memo.

The firm in September said it would "http://www.wsj.com/articles/infoblox-to-be-taken-private-in-1-6-billion-deal-1474289517" in a $1.6 billion deal. Earlier this year, Vista said it took private digital marketing company Marketo Inc. for around $1.8 billion and said it would acquire Cvent Inc., a cloud-based event management company, for $1.65 billion.

Large take-private transactions with high multiples aren't new for private-equity firms that invest in software and technology. With a limited number of larger private companies to buy and recent pressure to put capital to work, private-equity firms could do more of these deals.

Technology-focused funds raised more than $50 billion in each of the years 2014 and 2015, according to data provider Preqin Ltd. As private-equity players jostle to put this dry powder to work, this raises new risks firms will have to pay more to beat the competition.

The industry poured $55.65 billion into announced technology deals in 2016 through Oct. 12, and is closing in on the roughly $61 billion it deployed in the sector last year, according to data provider Dealogic Ltd. Technology businesses made up the largest share of private equity's global deal volume this year.

Thoma Bravo, another private-equity investor active in the software space, recently said it "http://www.wsj.com/articles/thoma-bravo-raises-7-6-billion-for-latest-flagship-fund-1473704895". Vista's latest fund has drawn keen interest as well. It held a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/vista-equity-sets-10-billion-upper-limit-for-new-fund-1458129602" raising an amount in excess of its 2014 fund.

Launched by Robert Smith and Brian Sheth in 2000, Vista got its start with key commitments from the A. Eugene Brockman Charitable Trust, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm in recent years has expanded into new areas, launching a strategy for smaller deals, a credit arm and a hedge fund along the way.

Opinion
Everyone's Worst Investing Fears; With assets in a funk, interest rates at zero and IPOs scarce, what's capital to do?

By Andy Kessler 
916 words
17 October 2016
03:48
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Snapchat is not about to disappear this newspaper "http://www.wsj.com/articles/snapchat-parent-working-on-ipo-that-could-value-company-at-25-billion-or-more-sources-1475778314" earlier this month that the firm may go public next year with a $25 billion valuation. Don't you wish you had invested at a $500 million value? Lots of investors underwater this year sure do.

Last month Perry Capital, which peaked with $15 billion under management in 2007, closed its flagship fund. Richard Perry, a Robert Rubin protg, "http://www.bloomberg.com/news/videos/2016-09-26/why-richard-perry-is-closing-his-main-hedge-fund" in a recent letter to investors that "this market environment has not worked well for us." Really? The Dow is now within a hair of its all-time high.

Some 10,000 hedge funds invest almost

"http://www.barclayhedge.com/research/indices/ghs/mum/HF_Money_Under_Management.html". More funds closed than opened over the last year. With $20 billion in assets, Lansdowne Partners is down almost 15% this year. The Rhode Island State Investment Commission is cutting hedge-fund holdings by half, following Calpers dropping hedge funds altogether last year. What's going on? This isn't a nasty bear market.

So many asset classes are in a funk. Macro investing, making bets based on major geopolitical trends, was all the rage over the last 25 years. But George Soros breaking the Bank of England is a thing of the past. Debt is now monetized rather than rationalized. There is a glut of commodities from forests to food. Energy is fracked. Private equity probably peaked a few years ago, but it hasn't yet marked to market. And bonds? When you have to pay Germany to own its sovereign bonds, it is tough to make money. That leaves stocks.

Equity investors have loved lower interest rates, because they make stocks more attractive than bonds and increase the value of future earnings. But at zero and even negative interest rates, it is a mess. Think of zero rates as a compass that can't point north and only spins around. Dividend-discount models to value stocks are driven by a discount rate that at zero makes every stock worth infinity. So stocks look cheap, even though they've never been more expensive.

What to do? The first rule of investing, unlike Fight Club, is that there are no rules. Investing is like fashion. What's hot and what's not changes at the whim of the market. It used to be every fund owned Apple, until it stopped working. Right now we are in what CNBC's Jim Cramer calls a Fang market, because of the power of Facebook, Amazon, Netflix and Google. Google has done the worst of the bunch since January 2015 up only 50%. Netflix and Amazon have both doubled. In an economy with 2% GDP growth, not much else works.

Tomorrow's fashion? If I knew for sure, I'd be on a Bora Bora beach. But I can point to the right neighborhood. It's the New over the Old. New productive companies destroying the old ways of doing business.

The market does the dirty work, providing access to capital for those it thinks will be the next wave of great companies. It starves those in decline. This is why BlackBerry doesn't make phones and why General Motors is throwing good money after bad into technology and partners for autonomous vehicles. SoFi is hurting banks.

But even being the New can be fleeting. Apple's iTunes stung music companies by offering reasonably priced songs instead of full albums. And now streaming companies like Spotify offer unlimited music for $10 a month. The Newer destroying the New. I call this Lily Pad investing: You've got to hop from one company to another before the first one drowns. But it's hard.

Finding the next wave of hot companies can be a pain in the assets. The minor leagues for this crop of the New is often venture capital. The New firms are then swallowed up by public investors during initial public offerings. Yet companies are staying private much longer. There is no public market for SoFi or Spotify. Thanks to "financial reform" acts like Sarbanes-Oxley and Dodd-Frank, the market for IPOs has been a ghost town.

Trillions in assets are itching to invest in these markets, but for now investors are stuck with firms that are under attack. Try to find investment in virtual reality, genome editing, drones, voice-control interfaces, artificial intelligence, chatbots or robots. Even immunotherapy and gene-therapy investments are far and few between. These companies become billion-dollar unicorns because they stay private. And not all of them will work. Hyperloop is aptly named.

Just recently, the IPO window has cracked open in a small way. Nutanix an integrator of servers and storage that hopes to destroy Hewlett-Packard, IBM and Dell EMC saw its stock almost triple in the first few days of trading last week. Earlier IPOs would sure be nice.

The king of the New, Uber founder Travis Kalanick, remarked at a German technology conference this summer, "I say we are going to IPO as late as humanly possible. It'll be one day before my employees and significant others come to my office with pitchforks and torches. We will IPO the day before that. Do you get it?" And that tells you why so many are showing such runty returns.

Mr. Kessler, a former hedge-fund manager, is the author of "Eat People" (Portfolio, 2011).

Pro Private Markets
Mulling Bold Bets, VCs Do More Vetting Via Science; Investors shaken by high-profile blowups seek out new means of due diligence

By Brian Gormley and Tomio Geron 
717 words
17 October 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Before bankrolling Freenome Inc. this year, Andreessen Horowitz took the company's diagnostic technology for a test drive.

Freenome aims to catch nascent cancers by analyzing genetic material in blood. Seeking validation, Andreessen sent the startup in South San Francisco, Calif., five blood samples to see if it could distinguish between normal and cancer-containing ones. Freenome passed the test, and Andreessen led a $5.55 million financing in June.

"They got everything right," Andreessen General Partner Vijay Pande said. An accurate test could detect cancers that are curable if caught early, he said. Mr. Pande himself is a former researcher in computational methods applied to biology and medicine.

Scandals at startups promising scientific breakthroughs or other innovations have refocused venture capitalists' attention on due diligence and prompted some firms to consider new means of vetting the people, processes and technologies of companies before they invest. While always fundamental to venture capital, due diligence takes on added significance as entrepreneurs are forcing investors to pick the winners out of a growing mass of likely also-rans.

Some technology investors are moving outside their expertise to search for the next massive hit, in hot areas like digital health, autonomous vehicles or artificial intelligence pushing the issue of diligence to the forefront.

Andreessen Horowitz's checkup on Freenome isn't common practice but it illustrates the lengths that a firm can go to validate a brand new technology.

With venture capital flowing, investors often feel pulled between deciding quickly when a hot startup is fundraising vs. ensuring a high-quality bet.

"Sometimes the more diligence you do the more you can talk yourself out of [an investment]," said Matt Murphy, managing director of Menlo Ventures. "You want to make sure you get the right people and the right strong signal. That's the art of diligence. It's not a volume game."

The game is more about marshaling resources to answer critical questions. In biotechnology, investors call on a growing network of research groups for hire in evaluating potential drugs.

Third-party evaluations help investors spot and kill programs that seem likely to fail. This is important because academic research is often tough to reproduce, according to an American Society for Cell Biology survey in 2014.

Founders Fund used outside experts when vetting cancer drugmaker Stemcentrx Inc., which aimed to kill tumor stem cells. AbbVie Inc. bought Stemcentrx in June for $9.8 billion after clinical trials showed the approach could work.

"Because this was a new field that we didn't have as much expertise in, and what they were doing was cutting edge, we brought in people from our network who understood the cancer space very well, and did diligence on the company very well," partner Scott Nolan said.

One market drawing increased scrutiny is digital health. Venture firms pumped $4.5 billion into the intersection of health and technology last year, up from $4.3 billion in 2014, according to Rock Health.

Tech entrepreneurs moving into medicine have fueled much of the growth. Yet health-care newbies are often surprised at the industry's complexity and long sales cycles, some investors said. One diligence red flag is the tech entrepreneur who won't hire experienced health-care hands for fear they represent old-line thinking, said Tom Rodgers, head of McKesson Ventures.

Startups need people who understand the politics and budget constraints of health-care customers.

"Strong commercial execution," Mr. Rodgers said, "will sadly almost always trump better technology in health care."

Diligence doesn't end once an investment is made. Venture capitalists sitting on boards guard against poor business practices from undermining the company. Michael Greeley, general partner of Flare Capital Partners, said he expects boards will go beyond standard financial audits and call in outside experts to review startups' technology and operations.

Investors want to push companies to adhere to best practices and erase activities that could lead to malfeasance or fraud. Adds Mr. Greeley: "The board doesn't want to be caught uninformed."

Global Finance: ATMs Try to Stop Wave of Cybertheft

By Robin Sidel 
625 words
17 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The ATM is the newest front in the war against cyberthieves.

A year after millions of U.S. merchants began installing equipment at the checkout line to accept credit and debit cards with security chips, the automated teller machine is getting similar technology.

The move comes as thieves increasingly target ATMs. While chip-enabled credit cards are expected to slow growth in fraud at the checkout counter, the number of ATMs compromised by criminals jumped more than sixfold from 2014, according to a recent report from FICO, a credit-score provider and analytics firm.FICO says the number of 2015 compromises was the highest it ever recorded, though it declined to disclose specific numbers.

The burst of ATM-related fraud also was the largest one-year increase since FICO started keeping track of such data about a dozen years ago. Meanwhile, rates of credit-card fraud, a more popular scheme for criminals, have largely leveled off.

The push to make ATMs more secure takes on added significance this month: Under MasterCard Inc.'s network rules, ATM operators onOct. 21 will become liable for any fraud costs that occur if a MasterCard chip-enabled card is used at a machine that isn't equipped with chip technology. That cost is currently borne by the card-issuing bank. Visa Inc. makes a similar shift next October.

The shift to more secure ATMs is expected to tackle the growing problem known as skimming, by which criminals rig a machine with a device that can steal a customer's card data, often including the personal identification number. The crook can then use the data to make a counterfeit card and drain the associated bank account.

The computer chip embedded in many new U.S. credit and debit cards fights such fraud by creating a one-time code for each transaction, limiting the ability of a thief to steal and replicate data.

"The U.S. market is definitely being targeted with card skimming," said Owen Wild, global marketing director for enterprise fraud and security at NCR Corp., one of the largest ATM makers. Nonbank ATMs account for 60% of recent incidents,up from 39% in 2014,the FICO analysis found.

Cyberthieves make at least 1.5 million illicit ATM cash withdrawals in the U.S. each year, according to consulting firm Tremont Capital Group. That figure represents a tiny fraction of the 5.8 billion ATM withdrawals consumers made in 2013, according to a Federal Reserve estimate.

Still, theft from ATM skimming can be more dangerous to consumers than credit-card fraud because the debit card is tied directly to a consumer's checking account. Banks will typically reimburse customers for losses, but the process can be complicated.

Cost is an issue for ATM operators; It typically costs between $300 and $700 to upgrade an existing ATMto accept chip cards, said Brad Daniel, president at America's ATM, a Plantation, Fla., company that maintains a fleet of the machines.

Everything ATM, an ATM operator and supplier basedin Brooklyn, N.Y., has requested 5,000 upgrade kits from a supplier but has only received 1,000 so far, said Jim Shrayef, principal at the firm. "I cannot get the product I need to satisfy the demand," he said.

So far, it appears that ATM operators are better prepared for the transition to chip cards than merchants that have grappled with challenges to get the equipment ready at the checkout line.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Bank of America Corp., which operate about 34,000 ATMs between them, say that most of their machines are ready to accept chip cards.

US bank earnings: 5 things to look out for

Ben McLannahan in New York 
1,023 words
14 October 2016
05:03
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Analysts are expecting a downbeat earnings season from the US banks, which begin to report third-quarter numbers on Friday. Combined profits for the big six   JPMorgan Chase, Citigroup, Bank of America, Wells Fargo, Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley   are expected to fall about 9 per cent from the same period last year, to $20.1bn.

Here is what to look out for.

The Wells effect

Wells Fargo s sham-account scandal broke in the second week of September   too late to have much of an effect on the books of the US s biggest bank by loans, which has ground out at least $5bn of net profits for the past 15 quarters in a row.

Still, analysts will be keen to get a sense of how Wells assesses the damage to its core retail business, in the wake of a flood of lawsuits and probes triggered by its admission that it opened as many as 2m accounts without customers  consent. And they will also be watching the other banks closely, to see how far repercussions could spread.

Analysts say those pressure-cooker practices were probably not unique to the San Francisco-based bank. Two-thirds of Citi s US branches (481) are within one mile of a Wells branch, according to Barclays. At BofA (2,471) and JPMorgan Chase (2,359) the ratio is about one in two.

Meanwhile, Wells   under a new CEO, as of Thursday   appears to have the most still to lose. The bank has slipped two places in the rankings of the world s most valuable by market capitalisation, while its credit spreads have widened. Changes in the hierarchy of the big banks are  infrequent but tend to be sticky , said Robert Smalley, a credit analyst at UBS.

The mood on Main Street

As ever, the banks  earnings season provides a good opportunity to take the pulse of the US consumer. Some of the specialists in subprime lending   such as Ally Financial, in car loans, and Synchrony, in white-label credit cards   have said that some strains have emerged, particularly in their lower-quality portfolios. But what about borrowers further up the credit spectrum? Are rising delinquencies limited to areas hit by the big fall in the oil price over the past couple of years?

Brighter debt trading

Banks  fixed-income sales and trading departments have pumped out mostly grim updates over the past five years   a period in which the total industry pool of revenues has roughly halved, according to Coalition, thanks to post-crisis regulatory curbs, a shift to electronic trading, and stop-start activity among clients.

But the bulletin should be better this time. Analysts expect Goldman and Morgan Stanley to report combined net income of $2.82bn for the quarter, 24 per cent higher than a year ago, thanks to greater primary issuance in the credit markets and tighter spreads.

According to David Konrad, an analyst at Macquarie in New York, Morgan Stanley is likely to deliver on its pledge of making at least $1bn a quarter in FICC revenues, increasing investor confidence that it will hit its main profit target of a double-digit return on equity by the end of next year.

The return of litigation risk

Deutsche Bank is fretting over a big settlement with the US Department of Justice over the alleged mis-selling of mortgage-backed securities in the run-up to the crisis. But for the big US banks, almost all of the Lehman-era matters appear to be in the rear-view mirror.

But that does not mean that new threats are not lurking. The third quarter saw 327 new securities-case filings, according to Lex Machina, an analytics company   the highest quarterly total since 2009, and about one-third higher than the quarterly average since 2011.

Meanwhile, many banks will be anxious about spillover effects from the Wells scandal. Many have pushed aggressively into new business lines in recent years, as they struggled to offset the profit-sapping effects of a flat yield curve. Do the incentives they offered salespeople now look a little dubious?

One bank already in hot water is Morgan Stanley, which has been accused of running sales contests in its stock-based loan business, pitting advisers against each other in a quest to convince customers to borrow against their investment portfolios. The Massachusetts Office of the Secretary of the Commonwealth Securities Division filed a complaint against the bank this month, highlighting what it saw as flaws in compliance and supervisory oversight.

In a statement, the bank said the complaint was without merit.  The securities-based loan accounts were opened only after discussing the product with each client and obtaining their affirmative consent,  it said.

Too-low ROEs

However low the valuation multiples attached to the shares of the big US banks, they remain huge profit machines. Since 2013, notes Mr Kotowski of Oppenheimer, the top six banks have combined to generate an average of $31.4bn of pre-tax earnings every quarter. In the first half of this year, just one of the six   Citi   earned more than Amazon, Facebook and Merck combined.

But such is the level of capital that the banks are now forced to maintain, returns to shareholders remain well adrift of many management teams  targets. This quarter, return on equity figures will be under the microscope again   especially now that banks have done most of their post-crisis spending on compliance.

 We re finally hitting an inflection point,  said Joo-Yung Lee, head of the North American financial institutions group at Fitch Ratings. Capital and liquidity are hugely improved since the crisis, but  profitability continues to lag .

Octavio Marenzi, founder and CEO of Opimas, a Boston-based consultancy focusing on financial services, said that banks would need to consider much more drastic measures   such as exiting entire lines of businesses   as they seek to boost their ROEs.

 There s very little activity at the moment,  he said.  Some of them are trimming here and there but nobody has really seen how bleak things are.

SoftBank and Saudi Arabia Team Up for $100 Billion Tech Fund; Partnership combines deep pockets with one of the world's most ambitious tech investors

By Alexander Martin in Tokyo, Alec Macfarlane in Hong Kong and Margherita Stancati in Dubai

Japanese internet and telecommunications giant SoftBank Group Corp. is teaming up with a Saudi sovereign-wealth fund to create a multibillion-dollar technology-investment fund, in a partnership that combines deep pockets with one of the world's most ambitious tech investors.

SoftBank, led by chief executive Masayoshi Son, is known for its bold and wide-ranging bets, ranging from Chinese e-commerce giant Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbanks-operating-and-net-profit-fall-in-fourth-quarter-1462862493". to U.K. chip designer ARM Holdings PLC, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-arm-these-chips-dont-come-cheap-1468827781". The ambitious plan lands as tech investors continue to plow record amounts of money into firms like Uber Technologies Inc., the world's most valuable startup at $68 billion, despite soaring valuations that have led many to worry that the sector is overheating.

SoftBank has also been stepping up its deal pace, pouring more than $45 billion into technology investments alongside co-investors over the past two years and putting its overseas investment operations into a separate unit earlier this year. Recent investments include participation in a $4.5 billion fundraising round for Chinese ride-hailing champion Didi Chuxing Technology Co. and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-to-invest-1-billion-in-koreas-coupang-1433313934".

To help bulk up its war chest, SoftBank has cashed in some winning bets, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-to-sell-7-9-billion-of-alibaba-stock-1464731957", in which it has a 28% stake, and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/tencent-agrees-to-acquire-clash-of-clans-maker-supercell-1466493612" for $8.6 billion. But it has also borrowed heavily to finance many of its investments and its debt currently has a junk rating.

The addition of the Public Investment Fund boosts SoftBank's investment firepower. The fund is central to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/saudi-arabias-cabinet-approves-economic-reform-package-1465252667". The plunge in crude prices since 2014 has hurt the finances of the kingdom, which depends on oil income for more than two-thirds of government revenue. Last year, Saudi Arabia's budget deficit was a record $98 billion, or 16% of gross domestic product.

The Saudi government is expanding the scope and size of the PIF, effectively turning it into a war chest for non-oil investments abroad. In June, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/uber-raises-3-5-billion-from-saudi-fund-1464816529", its largest overseas bet. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/saudi-arabia-approves-economic-reform-program-1461588979", which Saudi Deputy Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman estimated will eventually be worth nearly $3 trillion.

"The PIF is preparing for the twilight of the oil," said John Sfakianakis, a Riyadh-based economist with the Gulf Research Center. "They are looking for another success story like Alibaba."

SoftBank said its head of strategic finance, Rajeev Misra, will head the new fund, and that it has engaged former Deutsche banker Nizar Al-Bassam and ex-Goldman partner Dalinc Ariburnu for the project.

SoftBank, established in 1981 by Mr. Son, has a long history of investing in technology and telecommunications ventures. SoftBank was one of the earliest investors in Yahoo Corp., a bet that proved hugely successful and which the company largely cashed out of by the early 2000s. SoftBank first invested in Alibaba in 2000, when it put $20 million into the then tiny e-commerce company. Since then, "http://projects.wsj.com/alibaba/" worth $249 billion, making it Mr. Son's most successful bet.

During the past few years, Mr. Son has tried to give more order to SoftBank's investment operations, separating its overseas investments including Sprint from its domestic mobile businesses and putting it under the leadership of former Google executive Nikesh Arora. Mr. Arora "http://www.wsj.com/articles/arora-exits-softbank-pay-attention-to-the-deals-not-the-dealmaker-1466574258" after facing a barrage of criticism from investors.

After Mr. Arora's departure, Mr. Son has said he intends to invest in fields including smart robots, the "Internet of Things" and artificial intelligence areas he has said would be a focus for his company over the next 30 years.

Mr. Son has pledged big bucks elsewhere too. Last year, SoftBank formed a joint venture with India's Bharti Enterprises and Taiwan's Foxconn Technology Group to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-forming-joint-venture-to-invest-in-renewable-energy-in-india-1434981343", although it is unclear how far the project has advanced since its inception. Mr. Son has also said he wants to invest as much as $10 billion in Indian tech companies and around $4.5 billion in South Korea's technology sector over the next decade.

Limits to Putting Nobel Winners' Work Into Practice

The research that earned the Nobel Prize in economics this week has been influential in fostering the use of performance-based pay for top executives, but not all of its insights have been taken on board, experts say.

Oliver Hart and Bengt Holmstrm "http://www.wsj.com/articles/nobel-prize-in-economics-awarded-to-oliver-hart-and-bengt-holmstrom-for-work-on-contract-theory-1476093193" for their research on contract theory, or how to optimally design explicit agreements mainly between firms and workers to maximize productivity. The advice boils down to linking pay to performance where it is both measurable and verifiable and has a tight link to individual effort, as long as that doesn't sacrifice other important outcomes.

Their work has helped underpin the growth in performance-linked pay, especially in financial services, where outcomes are often easier to measure and attribute to individuals. Consider asset managers' returns, traders' profits or the fees investment bankers generate.

Tying pay to performance was such a hot topic, partly because of their research, that in 1993 President Bill Clinton signed a law preventing managers' pay above $1 million from being tax-deductible unless it was "performance based."

Mr. Holmstrm, "http://www.npr.org/2016/10/12/497637786/nobel-prize-winners-demonstrate-importance-of-contracts", pointed to the Wells Fargo & Co. scandal over accounts opened without customers' knowledge as evidence of "creepy and manipulative" incentives. "They thought they were designing a good scheme. &If you raise the number of new accounts we are getting, that's our business model. You get rewarded. And before you knew it, you know they probably created new accounts initially genuine accounts," he said. "But then, you know, they ran out."

The structure of pay in financial services was heavily criticized in the wake of the financial crisis, prompting the "http://www.fsb.org/wp-content/uploads/r_0904b.pdf" a stronger link between pay and risk-taking. "The bonus component of compensation has been much more variable upward in response to good performance than downward in response to poor performance," the global financial regulator noted.

Luigi Zingales, a finance professor at the University of Chicago, said the standards were "mostly window-dressing" that ignored the insights of Messrs. Holmstrm and Hart. "Their  informativeness principle' implies that in order to properly compensate people you need to put into the contract all the relevant variables, and this hasn't filtered through," Mr. Zingales said in an interview.

For instance, linking managers' pay to financial variables such as stock prices is "https://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/economic-sciences/laureates/2016/advanced-economicsciences2016.pdf" if the effects of factors beyond the managers' control, such as macroeconomic or industrywide factors, are stripped out.

"When I was a board member I found enormous resistance to it because managers like to be compensated for luck, and because they know when times are bad, job contracts won't be rewritten," said Mr. Zingales, who had served on the board of Telecom Italia.

In finance, it is much easier to measure explicit and especially short-term financial outcomes than it is customer satisfaction, team morale or risk- taking, for example. J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.'s multibillion-dollar losses by a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702303299604577326031119412436" highlight how incentivizing staff to focus on profits can come at the expense of risk management.

Richard Holden, an economics professor at the University of New South Wales and a former student of Mr. Hart's, said contracts linking pay to performance were rarely "optimal" for other reasons, too. "They typically don't internalize the potential costs of taking on more risk, either for shareholders, or especially in the case of banking, for the financial system and broader economy," he said points well illustrated by the financial crisis.

U.S. News: ACA Ads Clash With GOP Message

By Stephanie Armour 
475 words
14 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON -- The Obama administration is planning to use television ads and direct mail to boost participation in the Affordable Care Act's exchanges in the coming open-enrollment period, but its timing will mean fighting for attention amid the noise of the election.

The ads, which are partly focused on the affordability of coverage under the law, will run headlong into campaigns by opponents of the law who are using their own political ads to denounce it as a costly boondoggle.

Republicans seeking to maintain their House and Senate majorities have latched onto a sharp rise in premiums for many consumers to rally voters to their side. Their offensive may hamper Health and Human Services Department's efforts to sell the ACA as effective and inexpensive.

Open enrollment for 2017 coverage begins Nov. 1, a week before Election Day. Consumers will be able to browse the plans about a week before open enrollment.

"It's going to be incredibly difficult," said Kip Cassino, executive vice president at Borrell Associates Inc., which analyzes political advertising. "HHS is going to have a real problem until the election takes place. It's too intertwined into the political process."

HHS officials say they are confident they can use the experience of the last three years to reach people. Increasing enrollment, especially among younger and healthier consumers, is critical to stabilizing a market that has experienced higher-than-expected health-care costs. Those costs have prompted some major insurers to cut back or leave the exchanges, and led many others to hike premiums for 2017.

The Obama administration will increase its targeted outreach, HHS officials said Thursday in a call with reporters. The administration plans to send out 10 million pieces of direct mail, up from about 800,000 last year.

They will target people who were recently uninsured, lost coverage, or sought coverage in the past through HealthCare.gov or a state Medicaid program, officials said.

HHS also plans to contact the more than 20 million people who have visited HealthCare.gov, the federal exchange website.

The messages will note that financial assistance will enable most consumers who buy coverage on the exchange to purchase a plan with a monthly premium between $50 and $100. Subsidies blunt the impact of higher premiums for many consumers on the exchanges.

Officials said they didn't have details on how much the outreach effort will cost.

There have been 32,686 ads critical of the ACA since Jan. 11, 2016, according to an analysis by Advertising Analytics, a data company in Alexandria, Va. More than $20 million has been spent on broadcast television ads opposing the law. The company has identified about 4,058 spots talking about the ACA by the Democrats.

ICAP is stepping up its transformation into market infrastructure provision by purchasing the 70 per cent it did not own in Abide Financial, a Bristol-based trade reporting specialist.

Leverage Ratio Runs Counter to Policy Objectives; New paper from Federal Financial Analytics  predict ratio will have unintended effects

214 words
13 October 2016
21:19
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Oct. 13, 2016

The enhanced supplementary leverage ratio for banks functions as a "binding constraint for the majority of assets" held by global systemically important banks in the U.S., according to a "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/FedFin by Federal Financial Analytics Inc., a financial research firm. The Federal Reserve and two other regulators completed a rule on the supplementary leverage ratio in 2013, requiring bank holding companies and their bank subsidiaries to significantly boost the amount of capital they hold as protection against every loan, investment, building, security and other asset on their books, not just the risky ones. Compliance with the rule isn't required until 2018. The Federal Financial Analytics paper says the binding leverage rule runs counter to regulatory policy objectives laid out in the supplementary leverage ratio, and there are dangers that come with "divorcing total U.S. regulatory capital from economic risk." The researchers predict that the ratio will have unintended effects, including ones that adversely affect risk-taking, market liquidity and monetary-policy transmission as the rule "becomes an increasingly binding constraint." They also predict that it will lead to "accelerated migration of financial-intermediation services outside the banking sector."

Gabriel T. Rubin

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The venture-capital market may be thawing after a chilly winter, investors say.

U.S. venture-capital dollars invested in the third quarter of 2016 fell sharply from a year ago, driven by a drop in massive late-stage deals, continuing a trend from the beginning of this year.

Venture capitalists poured $11.7 billion into 956 deals in the third quarter, a drop of 41.6% by dollars and 5.6% by deals from the year-ago period, according to Dow Jones VentureSource. Last year during the third quarter, there were such large deals as Social Finance's $1 billion investment led by SoftBank and Avant's $325 million "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120151015ebaf01kvo".

Venture firms aren't having trouble raising funds, so investors are ready to make investments, despite the slow down at the late stage, said Keval Desai, partner at InterWest Partners.

Investors have been shifting their attention to new hot categories such as machine learning, computer vision, virtual reality and augmented reality, he said. "Early stage has been pretty healthy and deals are getting done at the seed and Series A level," Mr. Desai said.

However, there is less uncertainty in the market than earlier this year when there was a strong pull back in the public markets and in venture capital. The number of venture deals invested in the third quarter increased 6.0% from 868 deals in the second quarter of 2016. Dollars invested dropped 27.7% from $16.2 billion in the second quarter.

Health care was one area that has remained stable. Investors invested $3.2 billion into health care in the third quarter, up from $3.1 billion in the second quarter but down slightly from $3.6 billion in the year-ago period. The number of health care deals dropped to 186 from 196 in the previous quarter and slightly up from 181 in the year-ago period.

Dollars invested into later-stage deals dropped 45.1% from $12.8 billion a year ago to $7.0 billion in the third quarter, despite the number of later-stage deals dipping slightly from 332 to 324. This indicates that the massive later stage deals of last year have become more rare.

Early this year some tech company stocks in the public markets dropped sharply and the venture market also slowed, said David Yuan, general partner at growth stage firm TCV. But that has started to shift with more reasonable valuations for private companies and with recent IPOs such as Nutanix and Twilio, Mr. Yuan said. "I think we're in a transition phase here. We've seen activity pick up considerably."

Some rationality has returned to the venture investing market, said Ben Narasin, partner at Canvas Venture Partners. "People faced a lot of headwinds over the last three quarters," said Mr. Narasin. "But now we've moved into a calm sea with an occasional gust of wind."

Some trendy sectors are seeing very competitive deals, such as artificial intelligence, autonomous vehicles, insurance technology and security, Mr. Narasin said.

"All of a sudden just as we're feeling a little saturated with the mobile app economy, which was the driving factor the last five years, now we've got new market segments such as machine learning," Mr. Desai said.

At the seed stage, seed volume is very active, said Josh Felser, co-founder at Freestyle Capital. But the larger top seed firms are making less investments while writing larger checks, with seed rounds averaging $2 million to $3 million. This is due to companies needing to be larger and more advanced when raising Series A rounds, he said. In addition, there are many ways for companies to raise small "pre-seed" funds before coming to institutional seed firms.

"The volume of deals is down but there's so much seed capital flowing into the market," he said.

Big U.S. Banks Plan To Bail Themselves Out

By John Carney 
916 words
13 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Regulators want to prevent taxpayers from having to ever again bail out big banks. The latest idea: Make the banks bail themselves out.

Previously, banks had struggled to persuade regulators they had a plan -- called a "living will" -- that would allow them to be dismantled and shut down if they got into trouble without taxpayers taking a hit. Now, banks are creating new structures that would allow their most important parts to keep functioning, even if the parent company had to file for bankruptcy. The aim is to avoid the kind of market chaos that could cause economic harm.

To this end, there was a small structural change in the public portions of living wills released last week by the biggest U.S. banks. This involved the creation of a holding company to sit between the shareholder-owned parent company and its subsidiaries.

The new companies will house resources meant to support banking and brokerage units in times of crisis. J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. said it had created a new entity to fill this role, while Bank of America Corp. is using an existing subsidiary. Citigroup Inc. said it has similar plans.

The new entities will be a sort of emergency backup bank within the bank, a vehicle for internal bailouts.

The Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., the two agencies that must sign off on living wills, pushed the banks to pursue the new type of holding company in April guidance, according to people familiar with the matter. The suggestion came from an eight-word phrase in a 23-page memo that received little attention outside of banks, they said. The Fed and FDIC declined to comment.

The change could cause unease among regulators outside the U.S. It potentially could lead banks to keep less capital at overseas subsidiaries and make it less likely they would support all their global operations in times of trouble.

The upshot could be a push by non-U.S. regulators to require banks to provide additional support locally for subsidiaries. That would mirror the approach U.S. regulators have taken toward the subsidiaries of foreign banks.

"The best and most transparent way to deal with this would be an international agreement among regulators," said Karen Petrou of research and advisory firm Federal Financial Analytics. "But those efforts have failed. Regulators can't agree. Each, quite reasonably, wants to protect the interests and financial systems of their home country."

Critics of the new approach said the creation of new entities is a move in the wrong direction.

"They try to fight complexity with more complexity," said Cornelius Hurley, director of the Boston University Center for Finance, Law & Policy.

Moreover, to some the undertaking drifts far from the original idea for living wills, created by the Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law. "We're just lurching from one idea to the next, creating a jerry-built system of financial regulation," Ms. Petrou said. The wills were originally envisioned in the wake of the financial crisis as a way to make it possible for a big bank to fail without a taxpayer bailout.

The latest wills were drafted in response to faults cited by the Fed and FDIC in banks' 2015 plans. The stakes are high. The wills' process gives the regulators enormous power; in an extreme case, the Fed and FDIC could order the breakup of a big bank even before it runs into any trouble.

The wills now revolve around a scenario in which the parent holding company of a failing firm enters into bankruptcy. Meanwhile, its subsidiaries -- commercial banks, broker-dealers, merchant banks -- survive and are protected.

This is done by moving capital and funds to the subsidiaries, even as the parent holding company fails. That protects creditors and counterparties of the subsidiaries, a move it is hoped that prevents the kind of turmoil that followed the bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc.

Regulators support that general idea, but have been critical of how it would work in practice. Moving capital and funds between subsidiaries in a time of stress can be tricky.

When the parent runs into trouble, creditors and regulators may seek to prevent resources from being moved between a firm's various arms, regulators have warned banks. This is particularly problematic for global banks; foreign regulators or local creditors may attempt to protect resources to shield subsidiaries or branches in their home country.

Regulators in their April guidance told banks to keep sufficient resources in subsidiaries to deal with potential problems and be prepared for the parent company to deploy funds as needed.

Regulators cautioned this has its own problems. First, it is almost impossible to know where a problem will emerge and to have the right amount of resources at the right subsidiary. Second, keeping assets at the parent holding company increases the chance an outside creditor could try to grab them in bankruptcy.

That is where the new entities come into play. They will hold resources that can be rushed to support subsidiaries. But they won't have liabilities to outside parties, such as debt, and will be based in the U.S. That will make it harder for outside creditors or foreign regulators to try to grab assets.

The important thing is that this will happen now or in the near future, not when a crisis is a hand.

Aon Makes a Bet on Cybersecurity --- Exposure to potential hacking becomes a business opportunity for insurers, brokers

By Leslie Scism

12 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B6
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Aon PLC has agreed to acquire cybersecurity specialist Stroz Friedberg Inc., as more insurance companies and brokers aim to turn corporations' exposure to potential hacking into a business opportunity.

The companies didn't disclose terms of the deal in the announcement on Tuesday.

Stroz Friedberg was founded in 2000 by a former agent of the Federal Bureau of Investigation and a former federal prosecutor, both of whom worked on computer crime. The New York City-based firm, which has more than 550 employees world-wide, will augment Aon's risk-mitigation services, while also providing insights to help insurers expand and create new cyber offerings, the firms said in interviews.

World-wide, property-casualty insurers sold an estimated $2 billion to $3 billion of cyberrisk coverage in 2015, mostly in the U.S., according to industry estimates.

Cyber insurance can be obtained from about 50 insurers, with such purchases increasing by 27% in 2015, compared with the year before, on top of a 32% increase in 2014, according to a report by Marsh & McLennan Cos. earlier this year.

Aon is one of the world's biggest insurance brokerages and is a benefits consultant. Insurance brokers are middlemen who work on behalf of companies as they buy insurance, and many develop expertise in specific types of risk to make their services more valuable to policyholders.

They also work with insurers to help them design policies that address clients' needs.

Aon executives said the acquisition reflects an urgency on the part of the insurance industry to respond to clients who increasingly are worried about hacking that could damage their operations, as risk spreads far beyond the retailing industry and the credit-card data that many cyberthieves covet, to factory floors, power plants and even semiautonomous vehicles.

"There is a need front and center, in our face every day, and it is growing in magnitude," Aon Chief Executive Gregory Case said in an interview Monday.

Industry executives said there is a race within the insurance industry to find ways to help clients understand and model cyberrisk. American International Group Inc., and other insurers and brokers have made investments in or joined with cyberrisk specialty firms in recent years.

Marsh & McLennan's Marsh brokerage unit, a rival to Aon, has a collaboration with Cyence, a cybersecurity analytics firm, to help the broker's clients quantify and manage cyberrisk.

Within cyber insurance, growth was strongest among manufacturing firms in 2015, with a gain of 63% from the year before, Marsh said.

 Increasingly, wind turbine innovation is driven by system design, materials science, and analytics   all elements of the GE Store,  Jrme Pcresse, chief executive of GE Renewable Energy, said in a press release.  We, along with LM Wind Power, have a deep pipeline of technical innovations that can further reduce the cost of electricity. 

LM Wind will retain its management, headquarters, and 13 production sites across eight countries. GE intends to run it as a separate business that will continue to make motor blades for other manufactures.

LM recorded  ¬750m in revenue last year and it has 1,800 employees.

Mr Cooperman, one of New York s highest-profile hedge fund managers, has been outspoken about his intention to fight the charges.  But I refused to do so because of my belief that we acted appropriately and lawfully. 

Investors, including government pension plans, have publicly debated the value of their hedge fund investments in recent months, and have pulled more than $50bn from the sector this year, according to eVestment. That has brought total assets in the industry to just over $3tn.

Hedge Fund Research s HFRI Asset Weighted Composite index has risen in value by 0.5 per cent this year through September, compared with almost 7 per cent for a Barclays index of corporate and government bonds and 7.8 per cent for the S&P 500 index including dividends.

Mr Cooperman told Bloomberg on Tuesday that the SEC offered to settle the charges against him and Omega for $8m, but he declined.

Mr Cooperman has also said that the US Attorney in New Jersey s investigation of him and his firm is on hold,  pending the Supreme Court s decision in Salman v United States . Last week the Supreme Court began hearing that case, which could result in the biggest rewrite of insider trading law in decades.

The SEC has ramped up pressure on asset managers this year. The regulator said on Tuesday it recorded its most active year ever in 2015-16 for cases involving investment advisers or investment companies, with 160 such cases.

 By every measure the enforcement program continues to be a resounding success holding executives, companies and market participants accountable for their illegal actions,  said SEC chair Mary Jo White.

 Over the last three years, we have changed the way we do business on the enforcement front by using new data analytics to uncover fraud, enhancing our ability to litigate tough cases, and expanding the playbook bringing novel and significant actions to better protect investors and our market.

LM, owned by Doughty Hanson, the London-based private equity firm, is one of the world s biggest producers of wind turbine blades, and supplies not only GE s existing renewables business but also rivals such as Gamesa of Spain and Goldwind of China.

"https://www.genewsroom.com/press-releases/ge-plans-capture-more-wind-165b-acquisition-lm-wind-power-global-designer-and" unveiled on Tuesday is the biggest since GE created a separate renewables division following its  ¬9.7bn acquisition last year of energy businesses belonging to Alstom of France.  It shows GE s strength and commitment to renewables,  said Jrme Pcresse, head of GE Renewable Energy, adding that the deal would increase efficiency in its turbine making business and ultimately help drive down the cost of wind power.

About half of all new electricity capacity added worldwide last year was renewable, according to GE. Wind represented 35 per cent of the total.

The cost of wind power has been falling quickly as volumes increase. This is increasing demand but also brings risks for manufacturers if governments conclude that green subsidies can be cut.

GE is among a group of turbine and power companies that jointly pledged in June to reduce the cost of offshore wind farms to  ¬80 per megawatt hour by 2025, close to half today s average levels.

Zymergen Raises $130M for Engineering Tailor-Made Microbes

By Brian Gormley

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Zymergen Inc. has raised $130 million after selling investors on a vision for synthetic biology that includes recyclable electronics and coatings that better-protect cars and bridges from the elements.

SoftBank Group led the Series B round, which included new backers Iconiq Capital, Prelude Ventures and Tao Capital Partners. Return investors AME Cloud Ventures, Data Collective, Draper Fisher Jurvetson, Innovation Endeavors, Obvious Ventures, True Ventures and Two Sigma Ventures also participated. Zymergen closed a $44 million Series A round last year.

Synthetic biology involves genetically programming microbes to produce specifically tailored compounds. Zymergen, which uses robots and machine learning to speed this process, says its technology enables customers to improve margins on existing goods, accelerate product introductions and create novel offerings. It serves companies in markets such as chemicals, materials and flavors and fragrances.

Zymergen's financing highlights the potential investors see in synthetic biology to enable new products across various industries, including agriculture, foods, pharmaceuticals and waste management. In June Ginkgo Bioworks Inc., which designs custom microbes for customers involved in flavors and fragrances and other industries, raised "http://www.wsj.com/articles/synthetic-biology-startup-ginkgo-bioworks-closes-100m-series-c-1465385404"

Venture investors have also financed "http://www.wsj.com/articles/synthorx-raises-10m-series-b-for-novel-protein-therapeutics-1468987235" Inc., which uses synthetic amino acids to create novel biological medicines, and DNA suppliers Twist Bioscience Corp. and Gen9 Inc.

Emeryville, Calif.-based Zymergen has multiple ways to improve the economics of its customers' products, according to co-founder and Chief Executive Joshua Hoffman. The company, which can apply its platform to its clients' microbes, can improve traits in these bugs. It can improve thermal tolerance to make compounds perform better in a range of temperatures, for example.

Mr. Hoffman said Zymergen can't disclose names of clients but says it works with Fortune 500 companies and has had repeat business from multiple customers.

Zymergen also intends to bring its own internal products to market by working with its customers. They include thermoset plastic with improved degradation that would enable cellphones and other electronics to be recycled. Zymergen is also eyeing coating technology for paints used on cars, bridges and other metal objects that are exposed to the outdoor environment.

Although it isn't thinking of going public yet, Zymergen aims to build a public-quality board now, Mr. Hoffman said. Former U.S. Secretary of Energy Steven Chu is joining the board as an independent director. Deep Nishar, managing director of SoftBank Group International, is also joining the board.

Greylock Raises $1B for Latest Fund

By Tomio Geron 
436 words
11 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Venture investors show little sign of easing up on their fundraising.

One of the latest indications of flush times: Greylock Partners a backer of such highfliers as Facebook, LinkedIn and Airbnb has secured $1 billion in closing the firm's 15th fund.

Despite a slowdown in the venture capital investing market that began earlier this year, venture firms have raised nearly $34 billion through the first three quarters of this year, according to Dow Jones LP Source, marking a 25% jump from last year.

That pace could make this the largest fundraising year since 2007. Venture firms raised $8.8 billion in the third quarter.

Other firms that have raised at least $1 billion in the first three quarters of the year include TCV, Accel Partners, Norwest Venture Partners, Founders Fund, Andreessen Horowitz and Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, according to Dow Jones VentureSource.

Greylock, which closed the new fund in early October, raised its last previous fund in 2013, also at $1 billion.

Now with $3.5 billion under management, Greylock focuses on Series A and Series B investments, while reserving a smaller portion of the fund for growth-stage investments and doing some seed deals. In its prior fund, more than 95% of investments were involved in Series A or B rounds.

For consumer investments, Greylock has been betting on network-focused businesses, such as LinkedIn, GoFundMe, Nextdoor, Medium and Musical.ly, said James Slavet, partner at Greylock, based in Menlo Park, Calif.

On the enterprise side, the firm focuses on areas such as increasing productivity, revenue and security, backing companies like Cloudera, Okta, AppDynamics and Docker.

In addition, the firm is looking at emerging technologies and platforms, such as messaging, machine learning, robotics, virtual reality and augmented reality, to find the next Facebook or LinkedIn, Mr. Slavet said.

"The big question is, what will be the massive breakout companies in the next generation?" said Mr. Slavet. "It may look different from what we've seen before. The $100 billion question is thinking through what's next."

Greylock focuses on hiring investment partners with operating or entrepreneurial backgrounds. The firm also has in-house teams that help its startups in areas such as recruiting, public relations and customer development.

Recent Greylock exits include enterprise-software firm Apptio, which went public in September; Pure Storage, which held its IPO last October; Quip, which agreed to be acquired by Salesforce for $750 million; and TellApart, which was acquired by Twitter for $533 million last year.

Middlemen Fuel Insulin Price Rise

By Denise Roland and Peter Loftus 
990 words
10 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Insulin prices are soaring, creating pain for patients whose lives depend on the injectable drug -- yet most of the revenue from the increases isn't going to the drug manufacturers. It is largely the middlemen that benefit.

The major manufacturers of insulin -- Eli Lilly & Co. of Indianapolis, Novo Nordisk A/S of Denmark and Sanofi SA of France -- are collecting about the same or less than they did several years ago. The price increases -- top-selling insulins have more than doubled in price since 2011 -- reflect the growing role of middlemen known as pharmacy-benefit managers who negotiate rebates and fees based on list prices.

This convoluted payment system for drugs in the U.S. encourages high list prices and steep behind-the-scenes discounts, and is becoming a regular source of public outrage, most recently with Mylan NV's EpiPen, an emergency allergy treatment. The fragmented system offers some bill payers lower overall costs while uninsured patients and those with certain health plans pay more. Insulin, like EpiPen, is used by millions of Americans.

At the same time, higher overall costs are encouraging U.S. health insurers and employers to make patients contribute more to their health care. In part, their use of higher deductibles contributes to the cost shifting to patients. Higher drug list prices mean those patients most exposed to the price increases pay more before their plan coverage kicks in.

Drug companies have sharply boosted U.S. list prices for top-selling insulins -- Sanofi's Lantus, Eli Lilly's Humalog and Novo Nordisk's NovoLog -- since 2011, according to data provider Truven Health Analytics. Lantus, the top-selling insulin, now costs $248.51 a vial, up from $114.15 in 2011. A Sanofi spokeswoman said the company hasn't increased its list price in nearly two years.

"Prices for these products have increased," said Aaron Kesselheim, an associate professor at Harvard Medical School who has researched insulin costs. "It's also the case that there are more patients under high-deductible health plans and who may have a greater copay and coinsurance, and they're being exposed to a larger share of the prices as well."

Net prices, or what drugmakers retain after discounts, have stayed the same or fallen in the past two years as the pharmaceutical companies compete to offer ever-deeper discounts to stay on the preferred drug lists at insurers and the PBM middlemen.

The reason drugmakers sharply raise list prices without a corresponding increase in net price is that PBMs demand higher rebates in exchange for including the drug on their preferred-drug lists, said Enrique Conterno, president of Lilly's diabetes business.

Eli Lilly has raised the list price of Humalog -- to $254.80 a vial, more than double the 2011 price. After rebates and discounts, Lilly on average in the U.S. collects less for its Humalog insulin than it did in 2009, Mr. Conterno said.

PBMs get to keep a portion of the rebates off list that they negotiate -- though they pass the rest on to clients -- and the administrative fees that PBMs collect from manufacturers are based on percentages of the list prices.

However, counters Steve Miller, chief medical officer of Express Scripts Holding Co., the largest U.S. PBM, "We never tell pharmaceutical companies we want high sticker prices. We want a low net price."

He acknowledges that "certain patients get caught in the middle of this, and we have got to figure out how to put guard rails around that," such as setting a maximum pharmacy price.

About six million Americans use insulin, according to the American Diabetes Association.

For Sanofi's Lantus, the average U.S. net price fell by 17% in 2015 and is on track to fall a further 10% this year, according to estimates by equity researchers at Sanford C. Bernstein & Co. The firm expects the net price of Novo Nordisk's Levemir insulin to fall 6% this year. Sanofi and Novo Nordisk have warned investors that falling prices in the U.S. will hurt profit growth.

America's Health Insurance Plans, an industry trade group, said drug companies control pricing, and when insulin prices rise, "patients end up paying more -- regardless of what kind of insurance plan they have."

Christie Tucker, a 45-year-old electrical contractor in Port Angeles, Wash., said the bill for a six-week supply of insulin for her son Preston has soared in the past two years.

When Preston was diagnosed, Mrs. Tucker paid $40 for a six-week supply. But the cost jumped to more than $600 in January because, instead of a fixed-dollar-amount copay, her insurance plan charges 30% of the pharmacy price of insulin, she said. Mrs. Tucker has saved on some prescriptions using coupons but expects to pay $650 for the next refill.

High-deductible insurance plans are causing sticker shock for many patients. About 23% of American workers with employer-sponsored health plans have annual deductibles of at least $2,000 a person, up from 7% in 2009, according to the Kaiser Family Foundation.

Jeff Dunlop, a 41-year-old mental-health case manager in Winter Haven, Fla., said he paid about $1,250 for a three-month supply of Lilly's Humalog earlier this year. Mr. Dunlop, who was diagnosed with Type 1 diabetes as a child, paid the full cost because his family insurance plan -- offered by his wife's employer -- now has a $4,000 annual deductible.

He can handle the cost for now but said he worries about the future.

"There's a lot of people out there that just can't afford it," he said. "I'm one or two unfortunate circumstances away from being in that boat. That's scary because I need that stuff to live. Without it, I die."

Mylan Settles Medicaid Claims

By Joseph Walker 
677 words
8 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Mylan NV agreed to pay $465 million to settle allegations that it overcharged the government for its EpiPen products, the latest move by the embattled pharmaceuticals firm to quell the furor over its pricing practices.

The settlement with the U.S. Department of Justice comes after senators and federal health officials recently alleged Mylan had wrongly classified EpiPen as a generic product, when it should have been considered a brand product. The penlike injector delivers an emergency shot of epinephrine to counter severe allergic reactions.

The generic classification allowed Mylan to pay a smaller rebate on EpiPen sales to Medicaid programs, underpaying over five years by millions of dollars what it owed to the health program for the poor, Acting Administrator Andy Slavitt of the federal Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services wrote in a letter to lawmakers this week.

Drugmakers are required by law to pay rebates for sales to patients insured by Medicaid, which is funded jointly by states and the federal government. By classifying EpiPen as a generic, Mylan paid a smaller rebate of 13%, or about $163 million, when it should have been paying a 23% or higher rebate for brand-name drugs, Mr. Slavitt wrote.

On Friday, a Justice Department spokeswoman wouldn't "confirm or deny the existence of an investigation," and declined further comment.

Mylan, which moved to the Netherlands last year for tax purposes but is managed from Canonsburg, Pa., has been under fire over its repeated price increases on EpiPen. The company acquired rights the product in 2007, and has raised list prices by 550% since then to $608, according to data provider Truven Health Analytics.

Mylan hadn't previously disclosed any investigation. Settlements between pharmaceutical companies and the government aren't unusual, but typically occur after years of negotiations. The speed with which Mylan reached its rebate settlement signaled the company was eager to move past the latest controversy to engulf the company, Wall Street analysts said.

"You don't want this hanging over you, and there's a view that if you pay the fine, the public will just move on," said Ronny Gal, a Sanford C. Bernstein & Co. equity analyst.

Mylan said its agreement doesn't involve any admission of wrongdoing by the company, but that it expects to enter into a so-called corporate integrity agreement with the federal agency overseeing Medicare and Medicaid. Such agreements typically require companies to ensure they don't make similar violations again.

The company said it would record a pretax charge of about $465 million in its third quarter to cover the settlement. It also lowered its 2016 earnings forecast, largely because of previously announced changes in EpiPen access programs and the coming launch of a generic version of EpiPen.

Mylan has responded to the criticisms, in part, by saying it would introduce a generic version of the pen, priced at $300 for a two-pack. It previously blamed high patient costs for the pens on insurers who set high deductibles and drug-benefit managers who make money by negotiating bigger discounts off increasing list prices.

Shares of Mylan rose more than 10% to $39.66 in after-hours trading on Friday.

Mylan Chief Executive Heather Bresch in a statement called the agreement "another important step in Mylan's efforts to move forward and bring resolution to all EpiPen Auto-Injector related matters."

Mylan said it now expects 2016 per-share earnings, excluding items, of between $4.70 and $4.90, down from its earlier view of between $4.85 and $5.15. The company also reaffirmed its 2018 earnings target of at least $6 a share.

Ms. Bresch -- who testified at a hearing last month of the House Committee on Oversight and Government Reform -- has defended the price increases by blaming the current structure of the health-care system.

Mylan has cited insurers, which set high deductibles, and drug-benefit managers, which make money by negotiating bigger discounts off increasing list prices.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary

1,289 words
7 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B8
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Elliott's Nudge Good for Samsung

When it rains, it pours. Samsung Electronics suddenly finds itself juggling multiple problems at once, yet the latest drizzle from an old-time adversary could be refreshing.

Elliott Management, Paul Singer's New York-based hedge fund, is agitating for changes at the Korean tech giant that include a one-off $27 billion dividend, a split of the company and a Nasdaq listing.

The proposal, in an open letter Wednesday, comes as the smartphone maker already is embroiled in a massive recall of its latest phone due to defective batteries. Vice Chairman Lee Jae-yong, who has been running the company since his father was incapacitated by a heart attack in 2014, was appointed to the board last month, indicating a power transition.

Mr. Singer isn't a new face to Samsung. Elliott attempted, and failed, to block a merger between two companies in the group, Samsung C&T and Cheil Industries, last year. The merger was purportedly to help the Lee family consolidate control of Samsung Electronics, the crown jewel of the corporate empire, at the expense of Samsung C&T's shareholders.

One problem for the Lee family is that it owns only about 4.8% of Samsung Electronics. Throwing in stakes owned by other companies in the group, the Lees control a mere 18%. Analysts have worried that the Lees would push for mergers within the group to cement control while destroying value for minority shareholders such as with the Samsung C&T-Cheil deal. But this would be harder to pull off with Samsung Electronics directly, since slightly more than half of the company's shares are owned by foreigners, including institutional investors who could mass against such moves.

Anyway, what Elliott has proposed is largely consistent with what Samsung said it would do, though it is doing so at a slothful pace. The company has launched a buyback plan, but compared with the $58 billion in net cash sitting on its balance sheet, more can be done. The cash that the family would receive from a special dividend could help pay the huge inheritance tax bill in case the elder Mr. Lee dies.

And a split into a holding company and an operating company, as Elliott suggested, is also where Samsung is moving, especially since Korean regulators have been pushing for the company to simplify its circular shareholding structure.

A sorting out of the Lee family's holdings and a simplified Samsung conglomerate should help erase the substantial discount at which Samsung Electronics trades to peers. Samsung may have dragged its feet in moving toward a more shareholder-friendly structure. A nudge by Elliott should hasten the process.

-- Jacky Wong

Salesforce Should Definitely Leave This Bird in the Bush

Always hungry for growth, Salesforce.com now seems poised to bite off more than it can chew.

The cloud software company's name emerged two weeks ago as a potential suitor for Twitter. While hardly an exclusive list, Salesforce's appearance was surprising given the lack of obvious synergies and the cost it might bear to make the deal happen.

The potential cost got even higher considering Salesforce shareholders took 6% off the stock's value after the initial report.

That was only a tease. A Wall Street Journal report late Tuesday makes clear that Salesforce is still very interested. Chief Executive Marc Benioff has reportedly been talking up Twitter behind closed doors going so far as to describe the troubled microblogging service an "unpolished gem" at one gathering. Salesforce shares fell another 5% as a result.

In all, the prospect of buying Twitter has erased nearly $5 billion in Salesforce's market value.

Considering that Twitter probably wouldn't sell for anything less than $18 billion, that is worrying. Salesforce has zero net cash and would need to fund such a deal with a mix of debt and stock. That assumes Twitter shareholders would accept volatile Salesforce shares for nearly half their consideration in the deal. It should be noted that LinkedIn chose an all-cash bid from Microsoft despite the fact that Salesforce offered a higher price as the latter included plenty of stock.

Whatever Salesforce offers, it is worth asking what exactly the company would get in return. Salesforce provides businesses with subscription-based software to run sales, marketing and other functions. It is working to expand those capabilities with artificial intelligence, which requires reams of data to work. Mr. Benioff described data as "the currency in software's new world order" in an interview with the Journal over the weekend a good indication of why he is drawn to Twitter's 313 million monthly active users.

But Salesforce is already one of the few companies to have "Firehose" access to Twitter's real-time feed, so it isn't at all clear why it needs to buy Twitter outright. And Twitter isn't without its own significant issues, including stagnating user growth. Salesforce must demonstrate it can find solutions that have eluded Twitter's current management.

The prospect of losing out on another big deal may have Mr. Benioff convinced that he simply can't let this bird fly away. Investors clearly wish he would.

-- Dan Gallagher

Airline Price

War Proves

Cynics Right

EasyJet faces a hard winter. While U.S. airlines show signs of holding on to at least some of the benefits of cheaper oil, their European peers are proving cynical investors right by ceding them to consumers in a continuing battle for scale.

Europe's second-largest budget airline followed its June profit warning with another on Thursday. The shares nose-dived in anticipation of another raft of forecast cuts.

The problem is oversupply combined with creeping costs. EasyJet will fly roughly 8% more seats this year than last as a result of planes it agreed to buy in sunnier times and its strategy of taking market share from national flag carriers. But demand has been weak, so the airline has had to discount. Revenues per seat were 8.7% lower year over year in the quarter through September, and easyJet expects a similar performance in the current quarter.

The company didn't cite Brexit in its shopping list of excuses, beyond its currency impact. Instead it noted terrorist attacks and disruption at various airports.

One question concerns the willingness of Chief Executive Carolyn McCall to tough out the trough. But an executive thought to be her successor quit last month, suggesting she will stay.

The top five airlines account for roughly 55% of European capacity, compared with some 90% in the U.S., calculates Gerald Khoo at Liberum Capital. Everyone wants consolidation, but only through their own ongoing expansion. Time to buy a European holiday, not European airline shares.

-- Stephen Wilmot

Overheard

Surprises can sometimes sneak up on you.

Developed-market inflation still is undershooting economists' expectations, according to Citigroup's inflation-surprise indexes, which track the gap between forecasts and outcomes on a variety of price measures.

Either economists are getting more intelligent or, more likely, inflation is picking up because the undershoot has become the smallest since late 2012.

In fact, inflation is starting to be higher than expected in some surprising places: the eurozone and Japan. The former recorded the highest positive inflation-surprise reading in September since 2013. Even in the post-Brexit U.K., inflation is picking up, just not as sharply as thought.

Oddly enough, the country that is lagging behind is looking to actually raise rates. Inflation has been more buoyant in the U.S. than elsewhere -- but it still is proving lower than forecast.

Pro Private Markets
Welltok Raises $33.7M for Health and Wellness Tools

By Brian Gormley 
337 words
6 October 2016
12:34
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Welltok Inc. has raised $33.7 million in Series E financing to fuel the growth of a technology platform that employers and other groups use to promote the health of their members.

People use tools from digital-health startups, such as Welltok's CafWell system, to improve their health and rewards for activities they undertake to achieve their goals. Welltok has more than 70 customers, Chief Executive Jeff Margolis said.

CafWell collects information about a person's health and goals and creates a personalized action plan. It also provides reminders and tips for how to follow through with the plan and connects people with resources and benefits available to them through the sponsor, such as an employer or health insurer.

Those resources could include information about nutrition, managing a chronic illness, and improving emotional well-being. Sponsors can develop reward programs that encourage people to use these resources and improve their health.

Other startups doing related work include Chrono Therapeutics Inc., which disclosed a $47.6 million venture "http://www.wsj.com/articles/chrono-therapeutics-raises-47-6m-series-b-for-smoking-cessation-tech-1473362912" last month. Chrono's smoking-cessation system combines a wearable nicotine-delivery device with a sensor that reports when the device is in use. People who wean themselves off nicotine can earn rewards.
