Business
Elliott Management Seeks Changes at Samsung Electronics; Activist hedge fund cites  unnecessarily complex' structure of Samsung corporate empire

By Jonathan Cheng and David Benoit 
800 words
6 October 2016
02:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

Lee Jae-yong faces a shareholder vote this month on his nomination to the Samsung board. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated he was nominated to become chairman. (Oct. 5, 2016) 

Elliott Management Corp. is pushing Samsung Electronics Co. to reshape and simplify its complicated ownership structure, arguing the move would put the electronics giant on equal footing with its global rivals.

The activist hedge fund said in a letter made public on Wednesday that the "unnecessarily complex" structure of the Samsung Group empire is weighing on the shares of Samsung Electronics, the world's largest manufacturer of smartphones and televisions with a market value of about $230 billion.

Samsung Electronics is the crown jewel of a conglomerate controlled by the Lee family, whose companies play a dominant role in South Korea's economy, providing everything from consumer electronics to real estate, insurance and fashion. The various entities are linked in an intricate network of cross-ownership.

Elliott's proposal would essentially extract Samsung Electronics from the web by bundling its various ownership stakes in the other Samsung entities into a new holding company. The new Samsung Electronics operating business would be listed in New York and Seoul. Founded by famed stock picker Paul Singer, Elliott also wants the company to pay shareholders a roughly $27 billion special dividend and add independent directors. Affiliates of the $29 billion New York-based hedge fund own roughly 0.62% of Samsung Electronics.

A spokeswoman for Samsung Electronics said: "We will carefully review the shareholders' proposals."

Elliott's Samsung campaign reflects a broader effort across Asia to simplify complicated family ownership structures and improve corporate governance with the aim of improving value.

Activist investor Daniel Loeb, of Third Point LLC, has argued for similar changes at Japanese retailer 7-Eleven and Sony Corp. Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe has made governance reform a key plank of his plan to revive the country's stagnant economy.

Samsung heir apparent Lee Jae-yong, the 48-year-old grandson of Samsung's founder and a vice chairman of Samsung Group, has been restructuring the complex business and faces a shareholder vote later this month on his nomination to the board. Mr. Lee's ailing father was "http://www.wsj.com/articles/chairman-of-samsung-lee-kun-hee-hospitalized-after-heart-attack-1399792994" in May 2014.

Elliott's proposals echo some of the arguments that the hedge fund made last year "http://www.wsj.com/articles/samsung-suffers-symbolic-blow-from-seoul-court-1464679542" between another Lee-family controlled entity and Samsung C&T Corp., which holds a large stake in Samsung Electronics.

At the time, Elliott argued that the merger unfairly benefited the Lee family at the expense of smaller shareholders and urged broad governance changes at the companies.

Elliott narrowly lost the bitter proxy fight that lasted several months, though Samsung management vowed to adopt some of Elliott's suggested changes. Elliott's latest missive comes at a delicate time for the conglomerate, which is facing one of its biggest public crises in recent memory after a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/samsung-to-recall-galaxy-note-7-smartphone-1472805076" threatens to tarnish the Samsung brand. Even so, Samsung Electronics shares have continued to trade near their highest level ever.

The Seoul-listed stock has gained 45% over the past year and is 4% below its record in August. But Elliott said the shares are undervalued by as much as 70% because of Samsung Electronics' "bottom-tier" shareholder returns and "subpar" corporate governance. The firm said its proposed restructuring would bring the stock in line with that of its rivals by attracting a wider array of international investors. Samsung Electronics shares jumped as much as 5% in early trading Thursday in Seoul to touch a new intraday high of 1.7 million Korean won ($1,529). Shares closed 4% below their record high on Wednesday.

The new holding company, Elliott said, is a tax-efficient way for the Lee family to consolidate Samsung Electronics' ownership stakes in other Samsung entities. The family could then sell shares in the holding company to pay inheritance taxes in the event the chairman's death, people familiar with Elliott's plan said.

While Elliott is seeking talks with the company and the tone of its letter is complimentary of the management, it hadn't met with executives before releasing its proposed plan on Wednesday, the people said.

The Wall Street Journal

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A shortage of software developers in the U.S. has prompted some companies to seek talent in Africa, home to a young and increasingly tech savvy workforce.

International Business Machines Corp. has engaged young software developers in Lagos, Nigeria, to help build a data analytics business the technology giant is trying to ramp up quickly. The combination of an educated population and the proliferation of mobile technology on the continent makes Africa a good incubator of technology talent, said Leon Katsnelson, chief technology officer and director for IBM's analytic platform emerging technologies group.

IBM is building "Big Data University" to train technology professionals in its analytics tools through online training classes. Developers in Africa and elsewhere are helping write software to support the effort.

IBM isn't the only company that recognizes Africa's potential as an innovation hub. In August, Barclays Africa Group, through a subsidiary in South Africa, launched ChatBanking for Facebook Messenger, software to let local customers conduct bank transactions through the social network's chat feature. MasterCard Inc. runs a technology lab in Nairobi to incubate technology for digital payments and microloans for farmers. SAP SE has sponsored an "Africa Code Week" hackathon, and Accenture PLC is nurturing talent in Tanzania, Zambia and Rwanda.

Young technology talent can help companies create innovative products fast, Mr. Katsnelson said. "They don't have a vested interest in established technology."

Loyyal, a New York startup building a customer loyalty and rewards platform based on blockchain distributed ledgers, has hired a contract developer in Nairobi to work on its mobile app. Loyyal found developer Chris Ganga through Andela, Inc., itself a startup contracting firm with offices in Africa, New York and San Francisco.

"He has been absolutely outstanding," said Greg Simon, Loyyal's chief executive and founder.

Loyyal plans to put Mr. Ganga on core blockchain projects and may also hire two or three more Andela developers, Mr. Simon said.

He declined to say how much he has paid to Andela, but said the "cost is significantly reduced compared to a U.S.-based employee."

Andela puts applicants through aptitude tests to assess skills and problem-solving abilities, with fewer than 10% progressing to a round of interviews. Fewer still are invited to a two-week boot camp, where a series of technology challenges are laid out.

Pro Private Markets
Gormley's Take: Recursion Pharma Goes Off Beaten Path, Lures Investors

By Brian Gormley 
340 words
5 October 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Recursion Pharmaceuticals LLC's technology for discovering new treatments for rare diseases is cutting edge. But its business strategy is retro.

Having recently closed a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/recursion-pharmaceuticals-gathers-13m-series-a-for-rare-disease-therapies-1475494202" venture financing led by Lux Capital, Recursion uses artificial intelligence to find new uses for existing medicines. Instead of pursuing the conventional strategy of developing its own drugs in clinical trials, however, Recursion is using its technology to help large pharmaceutical companies convert existing compounds into rare-disease treatments.

That approach stands apart in an industry where most companies take the proven path of developing their own drug pipeline. Pharmaceutical companies are always looking to buy startups with a medicine that has shown promise in human clinical studies. Drug development is also a strategy Wall Street embraces.

In the 1990s successful biotechs such as Incyte Corp., which sold access to genomics databases, started out as service providers. More recently venture-backed antibody-discovery company Adimab LLC has revived interest in the technology-platform model, showing it can build a successful business by working with a series of pharmaceutical partners.

Recursion takes its inspiration from genomics companies of the past and modern businesses like Adimab. Through collaborations that give it a small piece of many drug programs, Recursion is betting it can do just as well as it could by owning all or most of a handful of drugs. Leaving clinical trials to collaborators also enables Recursion to concentrate on what it does best, technology and drug discovery, according to Chief Executive Christopher Gibson.

Recursion, which has alliances with three of the largest pharmaceutical companies, including one with Sanofi Genzyme, could eventually evolve into drug development as others have done before it. For now, however its focus is on using its technology and collaboration strategy to speed the introduction of new rare-disease therapies. As Dr. Gibson sees it, "We are trying to maximize overall impact."

In his brief tenure at Twitter, Mr. Morgenfeld served under two chief executives, most recently under co-founder Jack Dorsey, whose return a year ago brought hope that he would be able to revive user growth and advertising revenue.

In recent weeks, companies such as Salesforce.com Inc. and Walt Disney Co. have started to consider making acquisition offers, people familiar with the matter have said.

Artificial intelligence that can mimic human empathy and learn to answer complicated questions will be employed by "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/UK_banks" to serve customers around the clock and cut costs.

Luvo, powered by technology giant IBM, can read customer moods and respond accordingly, and RBS could switch on this ability in future.

 Like humans, Luvo has to be trained when dealing with new subject matter but, crucially, it learns from its mistakes and its answers become more accurate over time,  says RBS, which plans to unveil it at the end of the year.

The state-backed bank will be the first in the UK to launch a customer-facing service using AI after trialling it internally.

UK banks are only in the early stages of using AI but they hope it will improve customer service, because it can work consistently around the clock. AI could also allows banks to move staff from mundane to more complicated tasks, and to reduce the workforce and cut costs.

Warren Mead of KPMG said:  Of course it s about cost-cutting to an extent. Banks face a huge cost challenge as they go forward, but it can be done in a way that augments customer service.

For some processes that are  people-heavy and content-light , this could reduce costs by about 80 per cent, he said.

Using machines that can learn from their interactions with customers is the next stage banks are exploring.

 Obviously there are some streamlining and cost-reduction benefits. What we re seeing is organisations planning to use this to move staff from dull processes to more judgment-based roles,  he said.

Technology company IPsoft has built an  AI worker , or virtual assistant, called Amelia, which can learn and hold a conversation while responding to emotions.

Accenture is working with a number of banks to help them use Amelia for certain processes, such as helping people find a mortgage adviser or open a bank account.

The London borough of Enfield announced in June that it had recruited Amelia for frontline services, responding to residents  queries and helping with licence and permit applications.

RBS is offering Luvo to a small group of customers online in Scotland, before expanding it to NatWest customers in England. The bank is keen to emphasise that customers will not be forced to interact with Luvo, and human support is still available.

Chris Withers from IBM said:  As this cognitive system continues to learn over time, Royal Bank of Scotland will be able to expand Luvo s capabilities to more complex areas such as providing increased personalisation and using predictive analytics to detect possible issues before they arise. 

Mr Parker said:  Banks realise AI will be the future. It s not that the technology isn t there, it s that banks are figuring out how best to apply AI to processes and customer interactions.

FT REPORT - THE UK'S ENTREPRENEURS
Sale of homegrown pioneer puts spotlight on tech stars' strategy

By Madhumita Murgia 
980 words
4 October 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys TUE1
02
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology The sale of UK chip designer Arm was a jolt for a sector with high ambitions, says Madhumita Murgia

When shares in chip designer Arm Holdings ceased trading on the London Stock Exchange a month ago, the leading index of UK companies lost one of its star performers. The FTSE 100 company, whose chip designs are found in the vast majority of the world's smartphones, had completed its sale to Japanese company SoftBank for 24.3bn.

"A lot of people believe the deal shouldn't have been done at all," says analyst Richard Holway, founder of research company TechMarketView. "We would have much preferred Arm to stay as a UK-quoted FTSE 100 company. It's a great shame, they could have got whatever backing to do whatever they wanted to from the government."

Arm was one of the UK's original tech start-ups, founded in 1990 in Cambridge, where it remained as a tech ecosystem developed around it. The company grew into a multinational that some believe could be the next Intel, the US-based premier chipmaker.

It is not the first UK homegrown company to have been sold: in 2011 Hewlett-Packard bought Autonomy, another Cambridge company, in a deal for $11.1bn (although HP later announced an $8.8bn writedown of the value of Autonomy). In 2014, whizz-kid Demis Hassabis's artificial intelligence company DeepMind was sold to Google for 400m. Swiftkey, whose keyboard app predicts what a smartphone user is like-ly to type next, was acquired by Microsoft for $250m in February this year, and Twitter bought artificial intelligence start-up, London-based Magic Pony , in June for $150m when it was less than two years old.

The UK has probably the best record in Europe for creating fast-growth tech start-ups: a recent report by Mayfair-based investment bank GP Bullhound identified 18 UK "unicorns" - start-ups valued at $1bn-plus. In the first quarter of this year, the UK dominated the investment landscape, with more than  ¬1bn raised across 122 funding rounds; the emerging financial technology, or fintech, sector was the preferred area for investors.

Angel investor and venture capitalist Saul Klein, who runs seed fund Local Globe, says the UK has one of the world's biggest tech economies: "After the Bay Area [in San Francisco] and China, the UK has produced more billion-dollar companies in the past 10 years and accounts for more tech M&A than anywhere else."

But the UK roll-call of success still pales by comparison with the US, which is home to more than 100 unicorns. These include powerhouses such as car-hailing app developer Uber, which in June raised another $3.5bn and has been valued at $68bn, Airbnb, which is estimated to be worth $30bn, and Snapchat, valued at $16bn. Silicon Valley can claim three of the five most valuable public companies in the world: Apple, Google and Facebook.

Despite the entrepreneurial mojo the UK is building up, it still lags behind the US on at least one important question - when will Britain finally develop a tech company to rival the best in the US?

Niklas Zennstrom, who moved from Stockholm to London to co-found Skype in 2003, argues there is a delay because the consequences of the dotcom crash of 2000 played out more harshly in Europe than the US. "It was a nuclear winter until the whole ecosystem was reborn in the early 2000s," he says. "Compared to Silicon Valley, [Europeans] have never had that many generations of entrepreneurs and ecosystem-building of companies."

For many who have been investing in British start-ups for the past decade or so, concerns about how long it will take to reach this milestone come up over and over again.

Neil Rimer, co-founder of Index Ventures, a European venture capital fund, also advocates patience. "The clock only started running on this about 20 years ago, the US has had a longer period of time to build this," he says.

The UK's tech sector needs what he calls a "two-way street". This requires investors who are willing to take big risks and allow small companies a generous leash, as well as entrepreneurs who will need to trust investors when they advise them to refuse a big cheque and the temptation to sell out too soon.

So where might the UK's next big tech company come from? So far, the sector's biggest strengths have been in so-called deep tech - truly innovative technological advances - in software and chip design. However, innovation is also strengthening in new areas, such as fintech and artificial intelligence.

In the past five years, Britain has become a leading hub for AI research. "The UK not just has a lead but arguably is the best place in the world for AI given DeepMind is here and Apple, Amazon, Microsoft, Twitter have all acquired UK artificial intelligence businesses," says angel investor Mr Klein.

Though theFTSE 100 index has lost one UK technology company, it also just gained one.

Shortly after Arm's sale to SoftBank, enterprise software maker Micro Focus International entered the FTSE 100 as it engineered the absorption of the software assets of the much larger Hewlett Packard Enterprise. The deal has created one of Britain's most valuable companies, with a projected market capitalisation of roughly $10bn.

The UK's tech leaders have been cheered by the Micro Focus takeover.

Suranga Chandratillake, a partner at venture capital firm Balderton Capital and founder of Blinkx, an Aim-traded video platform, praises the "audacity and ambition" of this kind of deal.

"It's great to see a UK company do that," he says "It is exactly the inspirational role model you need."

Pro Private Markets
CD&R Forms Health-Management Services and Technology Platform; The investment is the New York firm's response to the nation's changing health-care landscape

By Amy Or 
698 words
4 October 2016
13:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Clayton Dubilier & Rice said it launched agilon health, a health-care technology and services provider that seeks to tap opportunities arising as the nation's health-care reimbursement system shifts from volume-based, fee-for-service reimbursement to one where services are rewarded based on quality, outcomes, improved patient experience and reduced costs.

CD&R said it formed agilon health after acquiring and merging management service organizations Primary Provider Management Co. and Cyber-Pro Systems Inc.

PPMC services more than 450,000 primarily Medicaid members in California; Cyber-Pro, through its subsidiaries, manages about 24,000 Medicare patients in Hawaii.

CD&R, which didn't disclose terms of the transactions in a news release, said Ronald Williams, former chairman and chief executive of insurer Aetna Inc. and an operating adviser to CD&R's funds, has been appointed as agilon's chairman.

Agilon health offers clinical and payment infrastructure and technology, data analytics and clinical models to enable physicians to manage care delivery and provide quality and value-based health care. The company contracts with insurance companies on behalf of physicians to receive a fixed percentage of the premium dollar or a fixed per-member, per-month fee.

This contrasts sharply with the current operating model where physicians and providers are paid fees on the amount of services they provide.

According to the release, Agilon health's technology, initially developed in 1996, is tailored to help providers with quality, case and care management needs, particularly regarding the increasing Medicaid, Medicare and populations that are eligible for both government programs.

The agilon investment is the New York firm's response to the nation's changing health-care landscape, with emphasis on containing health-care expenses while bolstering the quality of care.

Ravi Sachdev, a CD&R partner, said the opportunity set available for physicians and providers as a result of the transition is going to be much bigger.

"We aren't talking about the price going up by 4% or the volume up by 3%; we are talking about a fundamentally different addressable market," he said. "Instead of the fee-for-service dollar, physicians are going after $3 trillion of insurance premium."

But there is an economic risk to such a model: Physicians and providers need to be adept at containing the costs, or else the expenses incurred may exceed the lump sum they receive, resulting in a loss.

In spite of the economic risks, Mr. Sachdev said implementing the model gives providers a fighting chance to combat declining revenue because of the increasing proportion of government-subsidized patients in the system as a result of the Affordable Care Act.

"The patient population in [physicians'] practices increasingly have a higher portion of Medicare and Medicaid patients, and the price point of reimbursement on those lives in fee-for-service is lower than what they used to get for commercial," Mr. Sachdev said. "For the really sick Medicare population, they don't make much money today and don't have the appropriate incentives to manage them in a way that can impact long-term clinical and cost outcomes."

Bearing economic risks is something that physicians and service providers aren't accustomed to currently, and where CD&R said agilon, particularly with its technology offerings, will step in and aid providers.

Mr. Williams said when physicians have a large number of patients, it is difficult for them to keep track of issues that are crucial to the delivery, as well as the quality, of care such as "which patients have what conditions" and what are the state-of-the-art treatment protocols "based on patients' conditions."

CD&R said agilon serves about 1,500 primary-care physicians and 8,000 specialists. The company expects "exponential growth in coming years," Mr. Sachdev said.

"We are far into the value-based-care transition that while the market hasn't reached the full inflection point, we are past the concept of whether they are going to accept it or not," he said.

Pro Private Markets
Salesforce Agrees to Buy Marketing-Data Startup Krux; Deal worth $700 million will add to increasing AI capacity in so-called marketing cloud, Salesforce says

By Monica Langley

4 October 2016
04:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Salesforce.com Inc. agreed to acquire San Francisco startup Krux, in a bid by the cloud-software giant to bolster its marketing-data and analytics segment.

Salesforce is paying about $700 million, split equally in cash and stock, for closely held Krux, according to people familiar with the acquisition.

Krux last raised capital in 2014, securing $35 million from existing investors Accel Partners and IDG Ventures, alongside new investors SAP Ventures, Time Warner, Singapore government-investment fund Temasek and an investment vehicle of Japanese billionaire Taizo Son. The Wall Street Journal, citing people familiar with the matter, reported at the time that the round valued Krux at about $150 million post-money.

Krux is a six-year-old firm that provides companies with information enabling them to hone their marketing and advertising. The company, which already has a partnership with Salesforce, mines data across the internet and analyzes it to better understand and target customers.

Krux will supplement Salesforce's increasing artificial-intelligence capacity in its so-called marketing cloud, Salesforce product head Alex Dayon said in an interview.

Krux uses artificial intelligence to analyze trillions of signals to better identify audience segments for targeted marketing and advertising by its clients, which include big names from Ticketmaster to L'Oral, Krux founder and Chief Executive Tom Chavez said in a separate interview.

Salesforce, whose annual Dreamforce customer conference takes place this week in San Francisco, is known for providing cloud-based services for sales, service and marketing. In June, it "http://www.wsj.com/articles/salesforce-to-buy-e-commerce-platform-demandware-for-2-8-billion-1464781833" to buy another existing partner, Demandware Inc., for $2.8 billion. The deal added e-commerce to Salesforce's portfolio and was the largest acquisition to date for the company, which has stepped up its hunt for acquisitions as competition in the industry intensifies.

Ten fintech start-ups that are causing a stir in insurance

Oliver Ralph 
1,834 words
3 October 2016
04:52
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

For a long time, the fintech start-up scene took little interest in the seemingly sleepy world of insurance. Payments, foreign exchange and peer-to-peer lending grabbed the headlines, plus the funding.

But over the past couple of years, all that has changed. Insurtech   or instech   is now attracting entrepreneurs and the investors that back them.  Quite a few entrepreneurs who have been in other areas of fintech have moved over to insurance,  says Matthew Wong of research company CB Insights.

Funding for insurance technology companies rose from $740m in 2014 to $2.7bn a year later, according to CB Insights. This year has also been busy.  Deal pace is higher than it was last year,  says Mr Wong.  There s a lot more enthusiasm, especially at the early stage. 

The start-ups are targeting all parts of insurance. Many are focusing on distribution, using new technology to reach consumers that traditional insurers miss. Others are looking at analytics, helping insurers to use data to make better underwriting decisions. Blockchain   the technology that underpins bitcoin   is increasingly popular, while health insurance has been a big area of start-up activity in the US. Nor have start-ups ignored the potential of the  internet of things    the growing use of data-collecting devices in everyday items, from cars using telematics systems to connected homes.

Few start-ups have become full, risk-bearing insurers. Analysts say that the capital requirements, regulatory burden and complexity required, combined with the desire of investors for short-term returns, means that very few of them underwrite their own policies. The result is that for most start-ups, partnerships with existing insurers are critical. Without them, many would struggle to make the business model work.

Insurers tend to be willing partners, seeing start-ups as a way to win business in normally out-of-reach markets. But Nigel Walsh, partner at Deloitte, warns of drawbacks.  The risk is that the insurers will have no interaction with their clients,  he says.  They ll just be product manufacturing. 

Cuvva

Founded: 2014

Founders: Freddy Macnamara and James Billingham

Shareholders: Angel syndicate

Funding: 500,000

Based: UK

Activity: Cuvva takes a fresh approach to car insurance. While most insurers cover customers  vehicles for a full year, Cuvva breaks it down into smaller chunks. Its first product allows policyholders to insure themselves on a friend s vehicle for as short a period as an hour via an app. Its second, to be launched this autumn, will allow drivers to buy an hour of coverage for their own vehicles, which is designed to suit people who do not drive frequently. While Cuvva is still at an early stage of development, some analysts say that insurers will increasingly have to follow its model of offering more flexible products than their current range of fixed, year-long contracts.Cuvva uses data provided by the UK s Driver and Vehicle Licensing Authority to help verify policy requests, a process that it says takes about 10 seconds.

Cyence

Founded: 2014

Founders: Arvind Parthasarathi and George Ng

Shareholders:New Enterprise Associates, IVP and Dowling Capital Partners

Funding: $40m

Based: US

Activity: Demand for cyber insurance has been growing rapidly over the past few years, with high-profile attacks on companies such as Target in the US and TalkTalk in the UK persuading companies that they need cover. For insurers, cyber offers plenty of potential growth but also lots of uncertainty. How likely is it that any given client will be hacked   and, if they are, how much damage could there be? San Francisco-based Cyence is developing a system that can model these risks in financial and economic terms. It has already won its first customers, including Brit Insurance, AM Best and Marsh. In September it raised $40m from investors in one of the largest insurtech fundraisings of the year.

Lemonade

Founded: 2014

Founders: Daniel Schreiber and Shai Wininger

Shareholders: Sequoia, Aleph and XL Innovate

Funding: $13m

Based: US

Activity: Lemonade has been the subject of speculation. It first billed itself as a peer-to-peer insurer, but was coy about exactly what that meant or its business model. It has since raised money, signed partnerships with the likes of Berkshire Hathaway, Lloyd s and XL Catlin, and recruited high-profile executives. It even hired behavioural economist Dan Ariely, author of bestseller Predictably Irrational, as chief behavioural officer. In September the company, which unlike many other insurtech start-ups is licensed as an insurer, revealed it will sell home insurance in New York. It will group its customers together and, at the end of each year, any premiums that have not been paid as claims will be given to a charity of the group s choosing.

Oscar

Founded: 2012

Founders: Mario Schlosser and Josh Kushner

Shareholders: Fidelity Investments, Google Capital, General Catalyst, Founders Fund, Lakestar, Khosla Ventures and Thrive Capital

Funding: $750m

Based: US

Activity: Oscar is one of the older and more developed insurtech start-ups. Focused on health insurance, Oscar uses technology and data to improve the care it offers to its customers. It was created after the introduction of the US Affordable Care Act, known as "http://lexicon.ft.com/Term?term=Obamacare", and its services are available in New York and New Jersey. Among the benefits it offers are phone lines to its doctors and systems that help healthcare professionals manage members  care. It has some big-name backers, including Fidelity and Google, and a fundraising earlier this year valued the company at almost $3bn. According to Deloitte it has annual revenue of $200m.

PolicyGenius

Founded: 2014

Founders: Jennifer Fitzgerald and Francois de Lame

Shareholders: Revolution Ventures, Karlin Ventures, Susa Ventures, Transamerica Ventures, Axa Strategic Ventures and MassMutual Ventures

Funding: $21m

Based: US

Activity: Price comparison sites have been in the UK for many years, regularly assaulting potential customers with TV ads featuring meerkats, opera singers and other outlandish characters. But the US market has been slower to take off. Analysts blame a reliance on brokers for distribution, state-by-state regulation and wariness from customers and insurers. PolicyGenius is hoping to help change that, arguing that more and more Americans would rather organise their insurance online than via an agent. It offers a range of products including life, health and pet policies. With backers such as big insurers Axa, Transamerica and MassMutual, it could make a dent in the market.

Safeshare

Founded: 2015

Founders: Alexander Steinart and Christiano Pizzocheri

Shareholders: Founders and Z/Yen

Funding: 200,000

Based: UK

Activity: "http://video.ft.com/4584674442001/The-blockchain-Wall-Streets-latest-buzz/Companies" is one of the buzzwords of the fintech world, and this is no different among insurers. The technology allows transactions to be recorded securely in multiple locations. How exactly it can be used in insurance is the subject of much discussion within the industry. Safeshare is one of the few companies to have launched a blockchain-based product. It describes its market as  insurance for the sharing economy , and its first product illustrates what it is trying to do. In March it announced it had teamed up with Vrumi   a website that allows people to rent out their spare rooms for use as offices   to launch property insurance. The policies are underwritten at Lloyd s and the use of blockchain should, in theory, allow the parties involved in the policy to be linked securely and cheaply.

Simplesurance

Founded: 2012

Founders: Robin von Hein, Joachim von Bonin and Ismail Asci

Shareholders: Rheingau Founders, Assurant, Route 66 and Allianz SE, Route 66 and

Funding:  ¬20m

Based: Germany

Activity: One of the big hopes for insurtech is that it will break insurance down from the catch-all policies insurers have traditionally offered into more specific coverage for individual needs. Simplesurance is one of the companies aiming to do that by allowing people to buy insurance for products at the point of sale. Although smartphones, laptops and tablets dominate, it can be used for a much wider range of items. Founder Robin von Hein says the company has almost 2,000 ecommerce providers using its system, and that it launches in a new country every four weeks. Like other start-ups, Simplesurance is not a risk-bearing insurer itself. Instead it acts as a broker, passing on the risk to traditional players such as Allianz and Assurant.

SPIXII

Founded: 2016

Founders: Alberto Chierici, Renaud Million and Alberto Pasqualotto

Shareholders: Founders

Funding: Under 50,000

Based: UK

Activity: A relatively new start-up, SPIXII was founded by a group of former actuaries and computer scientists, one of whom used to be a consultant for high-frequency trading firms. Like many insurtech start-ups, its target is the way insurers interact with their customers. SPIXII is developing a chatbot   software that allows insurers, brokers and price comparison sites to talk to customers via programs such as Facebook Messenger and Skype. It is still early days, but SPIXII s founders say that within four months of launching it had a pipeline of more than 60 companies that were interested in its technology. It has also caught the attention of Germany s Allianz, which has put SPIXII into a five-month accelerator programme at one of its bases in France.

Synerscope

Founded: 2011

Founders: Jan-Kees Buenen and

Danny Holten

Shareholders: Mangrove Capital Partners, 5 Park Lane and Tiketitoo

Funding:  ¬5m

Based: Netherlands

Activity: Synerscope is one of a clutch of companies focused on helping insurers make better use of their data. Its chief technology officer, Jorik Blaas, has a background in MRI scanning and equipment used to look beneath layers of paint on old masters  artwork. The company aims to take the growing volumes of data that insurers collect   such as data produced by in-car cameras and drones   and turn them into something insurers can use. It has had some success in its home market, with insurers including Delta Lloyd and Achmea using its systems. Achmea used Synerscope to find out which road junctions had more accidents than others. That information can be used not only to reduce claims but also to prevent accidents.

Trov

Founded: 2012

Founders: Scott Walchek

Shareholders: OAK HC/FT, Anthemis Group, Guidewire and Suncorp Group

Funding: $46m

Based: US

Activity: Trov is another company aiming to break insurance down into easily digestible chunks. It sells item-by-item insurance policies for personal possessions, for whatever duration customers choose, via an app. Trov also hopes to improve the claims experience, using a chat service to automate the process. Despite being based in the US, its first market was Australia, where the service has been most popular with 18- to 34-year-olds. Like other start-ups, Trov does not do underwriting itself, preferring to partner with established companies. In Australia, where it launched in May, Suncorp is the underwriter. In the UK Trov s imminent launch will be Axa, while in the US   where it plans to launch next year   Munich Re will provide the underwriting, it announced in September.

FT REPORT - RISK MANAGEMENT: TECHNOLOGY
Insurers rush to grab hold of start-ups with potential

By Oliver Ralph 
724 words
3 October 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys RMG1
01
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Traditional players hope to take advantage of innovators' tech know-how, writes Oliver Ralph

Autumn started with a rush of deals. As August turned to September, a clutch of big insurers announced investments in start-ups. Allianz put funds into MoneyFarm, a digital wealth manager. Munich Re partnered with Trov, which is developing a range of on-demand insurance products. Axa backed Gasolead, which is creating a virtual assistant to help insurance agents generate more digital leads.

If this is an industry playing catch-up on tech, it is playing hard. "[Insurers] are working closely with start-ups rather than seeing them as a threat," says Nigel Walsh, a partner at Deloitte.

For others, however, there is a long way to go. Tom Butterworth of Silicon Valley Bank, which finances early-stage companies, describes the sector as archaic. "It's seen as the laggard in adopting new technology," he says.

The September deals showed the breadth of technologies in which insurers are dabbling as they look for new ways to help customers - both retail and corporate - manage risk.

Telematics is particularly popular. What started as the ability to assess how well young people drive via a black box in the car is quickly expanding into a range of similar ideas. Health insurance linked to fitness devices is one. Home or factory insurance policies linked to various types of sensor are another.

Data analysis is a hot topic. "The insurers are probably a bit more mature than the banks in using big data to drive value," says Roy Jubraj, managing director at Accenture. The idea is that, by analysing data, insurers can assess risk more accurately. This is not always straightforward. UK regulators recently looked at whether insurers' use of data could harm consumers, although it eventually decided that the issue did not merit a full investigation.

Meanwhile, blockchain, the technology behind bitcoin, is never far from the discussion. Insurers are investigating how it could make insurance contracts cheaper and more secure. "Blockchain has the opportunity to fundamentally change the way we interact with insurers," says Mr Walsh. He adds that smart contracts - which pay out automatically if something happens, without the policyholder needing to make a claim - are one way in which the technology could change the industry.

Yet there are limits to how far big, traditional insurers can go when it comes to making the most of new developments. "Legacy systems are holding them back quite a bit," says Mr Jubraj. "That's why major players are embarking on new platforms, modernising what they have."

Old technology, says Mr Jubraj, hinders insurers' ability to create new products quickly. "Today most insurers push digital services based on the products they are [already] selling. Not many are looking at you as a customer and driving a proposition around that, as Amazon has in retail," he adds.

That is where a lot of the start-ups come in. New entrants are selling variations on traditional policies - car insurance by the mile, for example, or cover for specific possessions. By combining with these start-ups, insurers win some underwriting business (very few start-ups are fully licensed insurers) and get an early peek at which sorts of products might work well.

"For the foreseeable future, barriers to entry are so huge that you'll see this layer added to the top of the industry," says Mr Butterworth. "It is the [insurers] most willing to integrate with the early-stage companies that will come out on top."

Cyber insurance is one exception. Multinationals including AIG, Munich Re, Allianz and Beazley have pushed into cyber. Analysts say it is one of the few areas of commercial and wholesale insurance that offers much growth. Adoption is much higher in the US than Europe, but EU rules coming in 2018 are expected to boost take-up.

However, customer demands are changing in cyber. While cyber policies traditionally cover companies for financial and operational problems caused by data loss, industry surveys suggest that policyholders have a wider range of worries, such as what happens if a cyber attack causes physical damage, or the consequences of a hack that affects products. The question for insurers is how quickly they can model and price those risks.

Body Founded: 2016 Founders: Alberto Chierici, Renaud Million and Alberto Pasqualotto Shareholders: Founders Funding: Under 50,000 Based: UK Activity: A relatively new start-up, SPIXII was founded by a group of former actuaries and computer scientists, one of whom used to be a consultant for high-frequency trading firms. Like many insurtech start-ups, its target is the way insurers interact with their customers. SPIXII is developing a chatbot - software that allows insurers, brokers and price comparison sites to talk to customers via programs such as Facebook Messenger and Skype. It is still early days, but SPIXII's founders say that within four months of launching it had a pipeline of more than 60 companies that were interested in its technology. It has also caught the attention of Germany's Allianz, which has put SPIXII into a five-month accelerator programme at one of its bases in France.

Synerscope

Body Founded: 2011 Founders: Jan-Kees Buenen and Danny Holten Shareholders: Mangrove Capital Partners, 5 Park Lane and Tiketitoo Funding:  ¬5m Based: Netherlands Activity: Synerscope is one of a clutch of companies focused on helping insurers make better use of their data. Its chief technology officer, Jorik Blaas, has a background in MRI scanning and equipment used to look beneath layers of paint on old masters' artwork. The company aims to take the growing volumes of data that insurers collect - such as data produced by in-car cameras and drones - and turn them into something insurers can use. It has had some success in its home market, with insurers including Delta Lloyd and Achmea using its systems. Achmea used Synerscope to find out which road junctions had more accidents than others. That information can be used not only to reduce claims but also to prevent accidents.

Trov

Body Founded: 2012 Founders: Scott Walchek Shareholders: OAK HC/FT, Anthemis Group, Guidewire and Suncorp Group Funding: $46m Based: US Activity: Trov is another company aiming to break insurance down into easily digestible chunks. It sells item-by-item insurance policies for personal possessions, for whatever duration customers choose, via an app. Trov also hopes to improve the claims experience, using a chat service to automate the process. Despite being based in the US, its first market was Australia, where the service has been most popular with 18- to 34-year-olds. Like other start-ups, Trov does not do underwriting itself, preferring to partner with established companies. In Australia, where it launched in May, Suncorp is the underwriter. In the UK Trov's imminent launch will be Axa, while in the US - where it plans to launch next year - Munich Re will provide the underwriting, it announced in September.

Insurers rush to grab hold of fintech start-ups with potential

Oliver Ralph 
704 words
3 October 2016
04:53
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCMA
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Autumn started with a rush of deals. As August turned to September, a clutch of big insurers announced investments in start-ups. Allianz put funds into MoneyFarm, a digital wealth manager. Munich Re partnered with Trov, which is developing a range of on-demand insurance products. Axa backed Gasolead, which is creating a virtual assistant to help insurance agents generate more digital leads.

If this is an industry playing catch-up on tech, it is playing hard.  [Insurers] are working closely with start-ups rather than seeing them as a threat,  says Nigel Walsh, a partner at Deloitte.

For others, however, there is a long way to go. Tom Butterworth of Silicon Valley Bank, which finances tech companies, describes the sector as archaic.  It s seen as the laggard in adopting new technology,  he says.

The September deals showed the breadth of technologies in which insurers are dabbling as they look for new ways to help customers   both retail and corporate   manage risk.

Telematics is particularly popular. What started as the ability to assess how well young people drive via a black box in the car is quickly expanding into a range of similar ideas. Health insurance linked to fitness devices is one. Home or factory insurance policies linked to various types of sensor are another.

Data analysis is a hot topic.  The insurers are probably a bit more mature than the banks in using big data to drive value,  says Roy Jubraj, managing director at Accenture. The idea is that, by analysing data, insurers can assess risk more accurately. This is not always straightforward. UK regulators recently looked at whether insurers  use of data could harm consumers, although it eventually decided that the issue did not merit a full investigation.

Meanwhile, "http://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Blockchain", the technology behind bitcoin, is never far from the discussion. Insurers are investigating how it could make insurance contracts cheaper and more secure.  Blockchain has the opportunity to fundamentally change the way we interact with insurers,  says Mr Walsh. He adds that smart contracts   which pay out automatically if something happens, without the policyholder needing to make a claim   are one way in which the technology could change the industry.

Yet there are limits to how far big, traditional insurers can go when it comes to making the most of new developments.  Legacy systems are holding them back quite a bit,  says Mr Jubraj.  That s why major players are embarking on new platforms, modernising what they have. 

Old technology, says Mr Jubraj, hinders insurers  ability to create new products quickly.  Today most insurers push digital services based on the products they are [already] selling. Not many are looking at you as a customer and driving a proposition around that, as Amazon has in retail,  he adds.

That is where a lot of the start-ups come in. New entrants are selling variations on traditional policies   car insurance by the mile, for example, or cover for specific possessions. By combining with these start-ups, insurers win some underwriting business (very few start-ups are fully licensed insurers) and get an early peek at which sorts of products might work well.

 For the foreseeable future, barriers to entry are so huge that you ll see this layer added to the top of the industry,  says Mr Butterworth.  It is the [insurers] most willing to integrate with the early-stage companies that will come out on top. 

Cyber insurance is one exception. Multinationals including AIG, Munich Re, Allianz and Beazley have pushed into cyber. Analysts say it is one of the few areas of commercial and wholesale insurance that offers much growth. Adoption is much higher in the US than Europe, but EU rules coming in 2018 are expected to boost take-up.

However, customer demands are changing in cyber. While cyber policies traditionally cover companies for financial and operational problems caused by data loss, industry surveys suggest that policyholders have a wider range of worries, such as what happens if a cyber attack causes physical damage, or the consequences of a hack that affects products. The question for insurers is how quickly they can model and price those risks.

Amino Capital Raises $50M Second Fund for AI and Big Data 

By Tomio Geron 
358 words
4 October 2016
00:43
The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Amino Capital, a firm focused on artificial intelligence and big data startups, has raised $50 million for its second fund.

Previously known as zPark Capital, Amino Capital has also brought on Huican Zhu, who formerly was an early Google executive and a chief architect at Tencent, as a general partner. Mr. Zhu, who has a Ph.D., from University of California, Santa Barbara, is a specialist in image search and Asian language search technology.

Palo Alto, Calif.-based Amino previously raised a $4.4 million first fund from among its general partners. Amino Capital II LP includes large Chinese technology companies as limited partners. The first fund made 39 seed investments, while the second fund will also invest in some Series A and B deals.

Exits include Orbeus, which was acquired by Amazon in 2015, Assemblage, which was acquired by Cisco in 2014, and Contastic, which was acquired by SugarCRM in 2016. Other investments include drone startup Skycatch, genetics firm Human Longevity Inc. and block chain startup String Labs.

The firm seeks to be the first check into a startup and often looks for companies that are spinoffs from university research. The firm looks for startups in artificial intelligence or big data because that is the expertise of its general partners, said Larry Li, a founding partner of Amino who was co-founder and chief executive at Cauldron Technologies Inc.

The firm also is interested in startups building on hard science, since its general partners have deep science backgrounds with Ph.D.s or other experience, said Sue Xu, a founding partner at Amino.

Besides its technology and business focus, the Amino team has strong connections with China companies and contacts and can assist startups in China. This can include anything from research and development in China to gaining customers and market position in China, said Ms. Xu, who was the founding scientist at GlycoMira.

The four general partners in the fund are Mr. Li, Ms. Xu, Mr. Zhu and Jun Wu, a former Google search executive and Tencent vice president.

Pro Private Markets
Recursion Pharmaceuticals Gathers $13M Series A for Rare Disease Therapies

By Brian Gormley 
428 words
3 October 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Recursion Pharmaceuticals LLC has raised $12.9 million in Series A financing on the promise that it can convert drugs idling on pharmaceutical companies' shelves into therapies for rare diseases.

Lux Capital is leading the round, which also included AME Cloud Ventures, Data Collective, Epic Ventures, Obvious Ventures and Wild Basin Investments. Recursion has raised $17 million in seed and Series A funding since forming in 2013.

Drug companies often stop developing products for strategic reasons or because the compounds were unsafe or ineffective in patients with a given disease. But a drug that fails in one disorder might still prove effective in another, and Recursion aims to uncover those indications.

By starting with a known compound, Recursion intends to cut the time and cost of finding new rare-disease therapies. There are about 7,000 rare diseases, or conditions affecting fewer than 200,000 Americans, according to the National Organization for Rare Disorders.

Recursion genetically manipulates human cells to create models of disease. Then it images the cells and uses machine learning to see how this manipulation affected the cell. This is like facial recognition, but applied to images of cells, Chief Executive Christopher Gibson said.

Mutating a specific gene might cause the cell's nucleus to become elongated and lead to certain other changes. Recursion examines about 1,000 parameters per cell, Dr. Gibson said.

Then it exposes the cells to thousands of chemical compounds to find ones that restore them back to their original, pre-manipulation state. Through imaging and machine learning, Recursion can also tell what cellular changes the drug induced.

Salt Lake City-based Recursion, formed to advance University of Utah technology, says it has partnerships with three of the largest pharmaceutical companies, including a deal with Sanofi Genzyme.

Other startups using artificial intelligence to repurpose medicines include NuMedii Inc., which has joined Astellas to identify new indications for undisclosed Astellas compounds. Some corporations are also interested in using Recursion technology to better-understand novel therapies in development, Dr. Gibson said.

Recursion has its own drug pipeline, including a treatment for cerebral cavernous malformation, which in severe cases can lead to brain hemorrhages. Instead of advancing them into clinical trials, Recursion intends to license these compounds out or create spinoffs that would move them into human studies. This enables Recursion to target many diseases and concentrate on its strengths in technology and drug discovery, Dr. Gibson said.

Tracy's Take:  Living Wills' Are In What's Next? The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Monday, October 3, 2016. 

2,450 words
3 October 2016
11:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Tracy's Take:  Living Wills' Are In What's Next?

Deutsche Bank, U.S. DOJ Continue to Discuss Mortgage-Securities Settlement

Current and Former MPS, Deutsche Bank, Nomura Executives Charged in Italy

Taiwan's Top Financial Regulator Steps Down Over Mega Financial

Sam Wyly to Pay $198.1 Million to Settle SEC Legal Battle

 Living Wills' Are In What's Next?

Saturday was a big day for a handful of U.S. megabanks: the deadline for filing revised "living wills" to the Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/regulators-reject-living-wills-of-five-huge-u-s-banks-1460548801" have been under orders since April to fix "deficiencies" in the documents, or else face sanctions.

Now the clock is ticking for regulators to judge whether their perceived deficiencies are fixed and the path ahead is a political minefield with huge implications for the future of America's largest financial institutions.

Living wills are supposed to describe how firms could go through bankruptcy without a bailout. Since keeping banks from being "too big to fail" has been a crucial goal of policy makers since 2008, these documents are more than just regulatory minutiae. Regulators' determination about whether the wills are credible, expected months from now, could dent the case for big-bank breakups, or bolster it.

The living-will process kicked off under the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law, which required banks to show that they could feasibly go through bankruptcy without taxpayer help. Big banks filed their first drafts in 2012, and new versions every year since.

In mid-April, the Fed and the FDIC said living wills filed by J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Wells Fargo & Co., Bank of America Corp., Bank of New York Mellon Corp. and State Street Corp. weren't credible or didn't facilitate an orderly bankruptcy. That finding triggered a provision of Dodd-Frank that requires banks to fix regulators' perceived problems or face sanctions, which can include higher capital requirements and eventually forced divestitures i.e., breakups. Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen"http://www.wsj.com/articles/yellen-calls-for-regulatory-relief-for-small-banks-1475071213" the Fed is prepared to impose higher capital requirements if it isn't satisfied.

The five banks were given until Oct. 1 to refile their plans. The timetable of less than six months left banks in a mad dash to fix regulators' concerns.

Some regulatory watchers have speculated that banks are playing a rigged game, where the Fed and FDIC won't ever be satisfied unless the firms massively shrink. But there is evidence regulators want banks to pass this test.

One very large bank, Citigroup Inc., "http://www.wsj.com/articles/citigroups-living-will-receives-passing-grade-from-regulators-1460548808" in April showing "http://www.wsj.com/articles/citigroup-wells-fargo-swap-bad-boy-role-1460577938". Unlike in the past, when regulators didn't give banks much feedback about the living wills, staffers from the two agencies held regulatory "office hours" this summer, as often as weekly, where banks could ask about topics such as derivatives and liquidity, according to people familiar with the matter.

There is also a political dynamic: If the five firms make significant improvements, senior regulators appointed by President Barack Obama may be able to cite those results as proof of progress before they follow him out of office.

On the other hand, giving all the banks a passing grade would open regulators to a credibility test of their own. The Wells Fargo"http://www.wsj.com/articles/how-wells-fargos-high-pressure-sales-culture-spiraled-out-of-control-1474053044" has "http://www.wsj.com/articles/lawmakers-take-more-swings-at-wells-fargo-ceo-john-stumpf-1475171906" just how massive these firms are, with millions of accounts and sprawling operations. Living wills contemplate the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-next-dodd-frank-headache-for-banks-living-wills-1456781482" of a huge, complex, interconnected megabank filing for bankruptcy, and ask whether that scenario would lead to economic mayhem. Imagine being the regulator trying to explain to Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) that the answer is "no."

Why the Yuan Still Has Long Way to Go to Supplant U.S. Dollar

ON WALL STREET
Worries intensify that Silicon Valley's debt binge has gone too far

By Tom Braithwaite 
787 words
1 October 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

If debt is your game, Silicon Valley is not your natural playground. This is a land of equity. Daring venture capitalists are looking for the next Google - and a tenfold return on their money. They do not want security, just a cut of the upside. In truth, though, the technology sector is not averse to borrowing when it can. Now it is on something of a debt bender - and there are some worrying signs that it might have gone too far.

Across the leveraged loan market , investors' desperation for yield has been great for companies: borrowing costs have hit record lows. Technology, though, is especially hot. Loans have grown more than a fifth in the past four years, faster than the broader market. Barclays notes that tech now accounts for almost 20 per cent of the S&P LSTA Loan Index, double its share of 10 years ago.

It looks likely to be getting bigger still. Eric Gross, analyst at Barclays, points out that a big proportion of the growth is tied to riskier activity, with the share of loans used for acquisitions and buyouts growing by a factor of six. This might be benign if profits had risen in lockstep. But they have not. For loans to software companies, the ratio of total debt to earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation has risen from 4.2 before the crisis to 5.3 today.

Lenders used to be put off by the "soft" aspect - there are few hard assets to seize and sell if the company hits the skids. Now, there is much more attention on recurring revenues - the sticky subscription model of "software as a service" that is supposed to prove reliable even in a worsening economy. It is not battle-tested, however. "That, in my opinion, is what is worrisome about this whole thing, that relaxation," says Mr Gross. "Even though those recurring revenues are a real thing, it's very difficult to quantify. Things change quickly in tech."

Part of the issuance is linked to private equity firms roaming beyond their traditional targets. Buyouts used to focus on tech companies that generate plenty of cash. Now, two private equity firms in particular - Thoma Bravo and Vista Equity Partners - are going after faster-growing, less profitable tech companies.

There is proportionally less debt but there is a good reason for that: the cash flows are so small. Thoma Bravo acquired data analytics firm Qlik Technologies for $3bn this year with a loan of $1.1bn. That is a lower debt-equity mix than a typical LBO that might use 75 per cent debt. Yet the company has just $13m in ebitda. It is a big bet on revenue growth, not the typical buyout thesis.

Even outside of an acquisition, some fast-growing companies are leaning more on debt markets. Uber is somehow considered big and stable enough for a $1.15bn leveraged loan at a mere 5 per cent yield, even though it makes no profits. If they are smart, more so-called unicorns will get in on the act. It saves the pain of another equity fundraising round: even the most desirable private tech companies, such as Uber, will find it increasingly difficult to find enough new investors willing to write ever bigger cheques to support a higher valuation. Going for debt rather than equity also spares early investors from further dilution, and buys more breathing room before a listing.

Even among earlier-stage companies, bank debt is featuring and showing signs of overheating. Silicon Valley Bank , which focuses on start-ups, is the de facto lender. It might add a $5m term loan at the same time as a young company does a $20m equity fundraising.

"As companies stay private for longer, they start to have business metrics - you're willing to lend more money," says Craig Becker, chief executive of SVB. Yet there are unnerving signs, too. For the past three quarters, SVB has reported an elevated level of charge-offs on loans, blaming "continued softness in early-stage venture funding".

Start-ups count on equity investors to give them more money to burn. Now, those venture capitalists are guarding their wallets a little tighter and entrepreneurs are burning through their bank loans instead. Mr Becker said SVB had reduced the portion of loans made to early-stage companies to about 6 per cent of its $18bn loan portfolio, down from 35 per cent in 2001.

The jitters in that part of the market, though, should be heeded up and down an increasingly debt-fuelled Silicon Valley.

tom.braithwaite@ft.com

Roundup of Form D Regulatory Filings--Sept. 30

By Zachary Cole 
465 words
1 October 2016
01:06
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

Building Engines Inc., Waltham, Mass., a commercial real estate app, has sold $26.5 million in equity. Launchpad Venture Group includes the company in its portfolio.

Karmic Labs Inc., San Francisco, whose mobile app helps businesses manage employee expenses, has sold $12.2 million in equity. In July 2015, the company received $5 million in Series A funding led by Greycroft Partners. Arbor Ventures, Camp One Ventures, Draper Associates, LaunchCapital and RPM Ventures are also investors.

Medicalis Corp., San Francisco, a developer of technology to improve how physicians use diagnostic imaging, has issued $4.2 million through an offering of convertible debt. The company previously issued $9.8 million toward an $18.5 million round of equity and rights, according to a 2012 filing. In 2008, HLM Venture Partners led the company's $7 million Series B expansion round.

OneRent Inc., San Jose, Calif., which provides an online property rental listing platform, has issued $700,000 of a $3 million debt offering. In March, the company raised a $4 million Series A round from Renren Inc.

ScrollMotion Inc., New York, a mobile app developer and creator of the first tablet editions of many magazines and books, has issued $5.6 million of a $13.4 million Series 2 round of equity, warrants and securities. A November 2015 filing showed the company issuing $11.4 million toward a similar round with a target of $12.4 million. Azoic Ventures lists the company in its portfolio.

SpotRight Inc., Little Rock, Ark., a marketing platform, has sold $2.9 million of a $4.5 million equity round. Access Venture Partners and Grotech Ventures are investors in the company.

Unmanned Innovation Inc., San Francisco, provider of an operating system for commercial drones, has sold $1.5 million in equity in connection with an acquisition. The company, which operates as Airware, acquired drone analytics software startup Redbird for undisclosed sum late last month. Andreessen Horowitz, Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers and Next World Capital are investors in Airware. Redbird's investors include ENGIE New Ventures and Groupe Monnoyeur.

Zero Motorcycles Inc., Scotts Valley, Calif., a maker of lithium-ion powered motorcycles, has sold $2 million of a $10.1 million equity round. Invus Group is backer.

Markets
Why Capital Standards Should Account for Operational Risk; The focus on stress-test capital has shifted attention away from the operational risks.

173 words
30 September 2016
16:23
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

September 30, 2016

The focus on stress-test capital has shifted attention away from the operational risks that banks face and begs the question whether U.S. regulators are taking an "overly-simple approach to regulatory capital," according to "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/FedFin "Operational risk-based capital aligned with improved safety and soundness creates incentives to reduce risk   no capital charge no matter how high can prevent risks like natural disasters," FedFin managing partner Karen Petrou wrote. Operational capital costs encourage banks to spend on resilient systems that can withstand operational risks, most of which cannot be prevented entirely (like natural disasters or terrorist attacks). The Basel approach to capital is based on a bank's gross income, though it would make more sense for it to account for investments in operational risk-management and mitigation systems, the paper concludes.

--Gabriel T. Rubin

John Stumpf, chairman and CEO of Wells Fargo & Co., "http://www.wsj.com/articles/lawmakers-take-more-swings-at-wells-fargo-ceo-john-stumpf-1475171906" on Thursday, Sept. 29, 2016. The hearing focused on problems with Wells Fargo's sales practices, including how unauthorized customer accounts and meddling in customer business snowballed over five years. Here is an unedited transcript from Federal News Service:

Chairman Jeb HENSARLING (R., Texas): The committee will come to order.

Without objection, the chair is authorized to declare a recess of the committee at any time.

The hearing is entitled "Holding Wall Street Accountable: Investigating Wells Fargo's Opening of Unauthorized Customer Accounts."

I now recognize myself for five minutes to give an opening statement.

We are here today because millions of Americans were ripped off by their bank and seemingly let down by their government. Fraud is fraud. Theft is theft. And what happened at Wells Fargo over the course of many years cannot be described any other way.

In fact, a whole host of federal laws were potentially violated, including the Truth in Savings Act, the Fair Credit Reporting Act, the Truth in Lending Act, the Electronic Funds Transfer Act, the Securities and Exchange Act of 1933, the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, and the Sarbanes-Oxley Act of 2002.

All charges must be thoroughly investigated and all culpable individuals must be held accountable.

And while the fine Wells Fargo will pay, roughly 3 percent of the bank's second quarterly profits, is tiny by Wall Street standards, the harm caused to consumers and employees is not. To the factory worker who just had her credit score dinged because of the fraud Wells Fargo perpetrated, the cost is big. To the waiter at the local diner, living paycheck to paycheck, who had to pay fees associated with a fraudulent account, the cost is big. To the Wells Fargo employee with kids to support, who lost her job because she refused to participate in the scheme, the cost is big.

HENSARLING: We will make sure those who were betrayed by Wells Fargo are not forgotten. It is in their behalf that our committee has launched an in-depth investigation, Mr. Stumpf, of your bank's activities. And let me be clear, today's hearing is just the beginning of our investigation. It is not the end. And as I speak our committee is gathering thousands of pages of records and documents from both Wells Fargo and the relevant federal regulators.

In the coming weeks we will be questioning Wells Fargo executives. If necessary, I will not hesitate to issue subpoenas because we will do what is necessary to get to the bottom of the matter.

Mr. Stumpf, we don't yet know what you knew when you knew it and what -- and what you chose to do about it. But we know it happened on your watch and we hold you accountable for the answers to why this happened.

At last week's Senate hearing you were uncertain of many matters. In the intervening days we trust that you have had a chance to refresh your recollection, to review your records, therefore we hope and expect you'll provide more complete answers today.

We need to know exactly when and how you and other executives at Wells Fargo found out about this endemic fraud. We need to know today what you directed others to do about it when you found out.

We need to know today who in management is being held accountable. We already know that as far back as 2009, former Wells Fargo employees started filing wrongful termination lawsuits, alleging fraudulent accounts, and improper sales tactics were taking place.

Approximately 5,300 Wells Fargo employees were fired over a five- year period for these improper sales practices, and perhaps as many as two million unauthorized accounts were fraudulently opened.

Based on these facts, we will also be asking serious questions of our regulators in the course of this investigation. If OCC had examiners on site at Wells Fargo during the time when these fraudulent accounts were open, and the CFPB was conducting regular examinations, why did it seemingly take "The L.A. Times" to expose the fraud? And once exposed, why did it take almost 18 months for the CFPB to initiate a supervisory review?

Today I don't know the answers to the questions. Perhaps our federal regulators deserve a pat on the back, but perhaps they deserve a swift kick on the backside. We'll find out which.

But we launched this investigation ultimately because it is our -- ultimately because it is both our job to hold both Wall Street and Washington accountable and to protect consumers from the excesses of both.

True consumer protection is the preservation of competitive, innovative, free markets that are vigorously policed for force (ph), fraud, and deception.

Mr. Stumpf, I know that Wells Fargo represents an iconic brand. I know that your bank has a very rich and proud heritage. I also know that you have hundreds of thousands of good employees who had nothing to do with this sordid affair, who do good work in building their communities.

But this sordid affair does remind me why I trust markets and I do not trust individual companies. And Mr. Stumpf, I regrettably have a mortgage with your bank. I wish I didn't. If I was in the position to pay it off, I would, because you have broken my trust and you have broken the trust of millions of others and it will be a long, long time to earn that trust back.

I now recognize the ranking member for five minutes.

Ranking Member Maxine WATERS (D., Calif.): Thank you very much, Mr. Chairman. And I thank you for agreeing to hold this hearing so that we can examine the fraudulent activity that occurred at Wells Fargo.

Mr. Stumpf, the word games stop today. Borrowing a customer's money without permission is not a sales practice violation, it's stealing. Using customer Social Security numbers to open credit cards without their consent is not wrongful sales behavior, it is identity theft. So let's call it what it really is.

Some of the most egregious fraud we have seen since the foreclosure crisis. For at least five years, Wells Fargo pushed aggressive sales goals for low-wage employees that were so unrealistic and so unattainable that some felt pressured to commit crimes just to keep their jobs.

It may have happened over two million times. Two million times. In fact, we have two former Wells Fargo workers, Julie Miller and Ruth Ladranverde (ph) today in the audience, who have borne the brunt of your choices.

Meanwhile, your senior management, board of directors encouraged, even bragged about behavior amounting to widespread fraud.

Today I hope you came to -- prepared to explain both how and why. While you personally told me you were prepared to take full responsibility, we have seen your testimony in front of the Senate Banking Committee and there are still answers that need to be given.

The testimony that we have witnessed in the Senate trying to explain what happened is not satisfactory, and we still do not have all of the information we need to understand how this happened, when the sales culture turned toxic, and who knew about it and when. Despite your statements to the contrary, any legitimate investigation shows that executives at Wells Fargo knew -- either knew or should have known much earlier than 2013 that these practices were taking place.

I think that executive conduct at Wells Fargo deserves a thorough investigation by the Department of Justice. Someone is responsible for the broken culture that led to this behavior, and (ph) needs to be held responsible; not the lower-level employees that have been left to bear the weight of the mistakes that have been made. This issue is personal for me. The size of Wells Fargo -- Wells Fargo's footprint in California means that many, if not most, of the employees and customers who were victimized by this are my constituents and neighbors. They don't deserve to have their trust violated by Wells Fargo, no one did.

I'm still receiving calls in my office complaining about Wells Fargo now and one caller described how he went into the bank, complained about excessive accounts that he knew nothing about, the employees called the police on him, and he was arrested; and yet violating the public's trust to drive up profits is exactly what Wells Fargo did. In the Senate hearing, it was revealed that even you benefit from that, Mr. Stumpf, your own bank account benefited from that deception. Now I know that you said you take responsibility for these practices and that you're conducting your own investigations, and that you and other managers are forgoing some of your compensation. That is welcomed, but let me be clear, it's not enough.

Unfortunately, this is not the first time we have seen abusive practices at Wells Fargo. We thought that you were working on these practices six years ago. Your mortgage executives said right here in this very chair, reassuring my subcommittee that you were committed to fixing Wells Fargo's forgery of mortgage documents, and yet we haven't seen the problem fixed, we have just seen it migrate to another part of your bank. So I hope today we can hear from you, Mr. Stumpf, because the American public deserves to know what happened at Wells Fargo and why customers are ripped off so blatantly and repeatedly. You can also rest assured that this is just the beginning and that we will be demanding more information until we get to the bottom of this.

And of course, I urge your cooperation and I must tell you that I have known you for a while, I have communicated with you. At times you have been very helpful to my constituents, so I'm very disappointed and we must get to the bottom of this.

And I want to be able to receive the documents and information that we requested from you, I'm told that they have been refused. I think it is to your best interest to come forward with those documents.

Mr. Chairman, I yield back the balance of my time.

Wells Fargo CEO John STUMPF: (OFF-MIKE)

HENSARLING: Thank you, please be seated.
